Sue Chapman, the pool's manager, said those who became sick swam in the pool between 19 and 26 Jun [2016].\n\nThe whole centre would be closed for 2 days for cleaning of the arena's leisure pool, lane pool, spa and toddler pools. A higher-than-usual dose of chlorine dioxide would be used. 'Although we have not been instructed by Regional Public Health to close, we believe the health of our customers is paramount,' Chapman said.\n\n'We apologise for the inconvenience to our regular users, but expect they would rather we took this precautionary approach.' Chapman said and surfaces and pool equipment would also be cleaned. 'Anything the water has touched will be washed.'\n\nThe pool is scheduled to reopen at 5am on Monday [25 Jul 2016].\n\nThe council's statement said it was important people did not swim for at least a fortnight [2 weeks] after any illness involving diarrhoea and tummy upset, to avoid passing bugs to other swimmers. People were also reminded to shower before entering the pool and to ensure babies and toddlers wore a swim suit and, if required, a swim nappy that was tight-fitting around the legs.\n\nWellington City Council's recreation facilities manager Julian Todd also asked people to stay away from the capital city's pools if they have been sick with diarrhoea or stomach upsets in the past couple of weeks.\n\nStaff at Wellington's council pools will work closely with Regional Public Health and with Porirua City Council to keep an eye on the new cryptosporidiosis outbreak, Todd said.\n\n'But our main advice is please don't come to our pools if you've been sick in the past fortnight. If you do you'll just put the health of other people at risk and also possibly cause our pools to be closed.'\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Infection with the intestinal protozoan _Cryptosporidium_ causes cryptosporidiosis, an acute gastrointestinal infection with diarrhoea and vomiting. In healthy individuals the infection is usually self-limiting. However, in immunosuppressed patients the course may be severe and require hospital admission. Transmission in swimming pools is well known (see previous ProMED-mail reports below). Cryptosporidium is not very sensitive to chlorination and if children -- as pointed out here -- do not wear 'swim nappies,' they should not enter the pool. - Mod.EP\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/24203.]", "summary": "If you do you'll just put the health of other people at risk and also possibly cause our pools to be closed.'\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Infection with the intestinal protozoan _Cryptosporidium_ causes cryptosporidiosis, an acute gastrointestinal infection with diarrhoea and vomiting. A total of 29 cases of cryptosporidiosis, a disease caused by a microscopic parasite resistant to chlorination which causes sickness and diarrhoea, have so far been confirmed.\n\nTwo cases of bacterial infection _E. coli_ 0157, which can cause severe stomach pain and bloody diarrhoea and can affect kidney function, have also been reported. All those affected visited Swithens Farm from the beginning of March [2016] onwards.\n\nPublic Health England (PHE) has been drafted in to deal with the issue and the owners of the farm voluntarily closed the facility for a period after the outbreak became apparent.\n\nThe petting farm has reopened and its owners say 'every effort is being taken to ensure that visitors are not put at risk'.\n\nWe are working closely with Leeds City Council and Animal and Plant Health Agency to investigate further and to advise the premises concerned. Dr Mike Gent, consultant in communicable disease control with Public Health England. Dr Mike Gent, from PHE, said: 'We are working closely with Leeds City Council and Animal and Plant Health Agency to investigate further and to advise the premises concerned.'\n\nHe said the farm was not allowed to reopen 'until a number of matters had been addressed' and urged visitors to such attractions to wash their hands after touching animals.\n\nThe infections can be the result of contact with animals carrying germs or parasites, and often peak in spring. Between January and May 2015 there were at least 7 outbreaks of cryptosporidiosis linked to petting farms across England, affecting around 130 people.\n\nIan and Angela Broadhead, who run Swithens Farm, have released a statement explaining that they are working with the council after becoming aware that 'there have been some children with upset stomachs'. They said: 'The farm is open to the public and every effort is being taken to ensure that visitors are not put at risk.'\n\n[Byline: Jonathan Brown]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Cryptosporidia and indeed _E. coli_ are common microorganisms of animals including animals at petting farms. It is important to note they are not saying to avoid the summer pastures, just stressing the importance of washing your hands immediately after contact with the cows.\n\nCryptosporidium, or Crypto, is a microscopic parasite causing a diarrheal illness. Common symptoms include diarrhea, stomach cramps or pain, dehydration (which is often most serious in the very young and the very old), nausea, vomiting, fever and weight loss. Symptoms usually begin 2 to 10 days after infection and normally last 1-2 weeks. Once a person is infected, the parasites live in the intestines and are passed in the stool.\n\nCDC guidelines pro prevention of infection with Cryptosporidium can be found here:\nhttp://www.cdc.gov/parasites/crypto/prevention.html\n\n[Byline: Robert Herriman]\n\n--\nCommunicated by;\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Calves are well-known reservoirs of _Cryptosporidium_ spp. The hypothesis offered above that the springtime release of calves to summer pastures results in sudden spread of Cryptosporidia on the pastures is interesting. PLoS Negl Trop Dis. 2016;10(4):e0004534, available at http://dx.doi.org/10.1371/journal.pntd.0004534] and I would like to offer a clarification.\n\nThe Moderator is correct that _Cryptosporidium_ spp. is not a tropical parasite. Many news agencies reported our findings with a similar headline, but this was not suggested in our report. Rather, this outbreak investigation provides 2 new insights:\n\nFirst, we detail the sustained transmission of human cryptosporidiosis in the Arctic, moving across 10 remote villages over a period of more than 1 year, in a region where this disease was not previously known. The annual incidence rates (IR) in Nunavik for 2013 and 2014 were respectively 322.5 and 246.4 / 100 000 persons, compared to an IR of 2.4 / 100 000 persons for Canada as a whole in 2013.\n\nSecond, the identification by molecular typing of _C. hominis_ subtype Id in all specimens tested rules-out zoonotic transmission for these specimens, though the possibility remains that other _Cryptosporidium_ subtypes could have been present in specimens that were not characterised. Anthroponotic genospecies of _Cryptosporidium_, with epidemiologic features that suggest sustained person-to-person transmission, are more typically seen in low-income countries than elsewhere in North-America.\n\nSporadic human infections with subtype Id have previously been reported in Ontario and British Columbia, but not in Arctic ecosystems. No animal-derived subtype Id strains have been described. To the best of our knowledge, the _C. hominis_ 'Id subtypes' identified in this study have not been reported previously in Canada.\n\nThere are no farm livestock animals in most Arctic regions. However, in Nunavik, _Cryptosporidium_ spp. has been previously detected in ringed seals, bearded seals and blue mussels, making food-borne transmission through the consumption of marine animals a possible route of infection. Elsewhere in Arctic North America, dogs and caribou have been found to carry _Cryptosporidium_ and may be another source of zoonotic infection. Indeed, in contrast to the current report, the only other study involving the molecular characterization of _Cryptosporidium_ infections in humans in the Arctic, reported only _C. parvum IIa_ in diarrhoeic patients in Nunavut [Iqbal A, Goldfarb DM, Slinger R and Dixon BR.; Prevalence and molecular characterization of _Cryptosporidium_ spp. and _Giardia duodenalis_ in diarrhoeic patients in the Qikiqtani Region, Nunavut, Canada. Int J Circumpolar Health. 2015;74, available at http://www.circumpolarhealthjournal.net/index.php/ijch/article/view/27713]\n\nThe high IR of the Nunavik outbreak and the recent recognition of widespread human cryptosporidiosis in neighboring Nunavut are cause for concern about the possible long-term effects on growth and development of children in Inuit communities facing myriad other challenges. Though repeated enteric infections are thought to be prevalent in these regions, little data currently inform our understanding of their clinical burden and etiologic spectrum. Such data are needed for the development of preventive strategies that take into account human practices and environmental changes that disproportionately affect the Arctic.\n\n--\nCedric Yansouni\nDivision of Infectious Diseases,\nDepartment of Medical Microbiology\nMcGill University Health Centre, Montr\u00e9al\nCanada\n<cpyansouni@yahoo.ca>\n\n[ProMED thanks Professor Cedric Yansouni for this clarifying comments. Public health officials say that 157 cases have been reported by DOH-Hillsborough in the last 2 months. Last year [2013], there were only 11 cases during the same time period.\n\nMany of those sickened spent time at day care or schools during the incubation period. Most of those sickened are under the age of 15.\n\n_Cryptosporidium_ is a parasite that is passed through infected feces. It is spread after improper hand washing after using the bathroom or changing diapers. It spreads easily in chlorinated swimming pools and through hand-to-mouth contact.\n\nBecause of this alarming increase in the case count, officials are asking the public to help prevent the spread of this disease. Practice good hygiene at all times. Always wash your hands with soap and water after using the toilet, before and after taking care of someone who is ill, and after changing diapers. Wash your hands before and during food preparation. And remember that hand sanitizers are not effective against crypto; only soap and water work well. No one who has been ill with diarrhea should swim in pools, enter splash pads or hot tubs, or go into a spa until two weeks have passed after complete recovery.\n\nWhen you are at a pool, take a shower before you enter. Only change diapers in the bathroom; never at poolside. Take children on frequent bathroom breaks while they are swimming. Wash hands well after contact with animals. And stay home when you are sick with diarrhea or vomiting.\n\n[Byline: Linda Larsen]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Outbreaks of _Cryptosporidium_ are frequent in the United States, and are usually related to exposure in swimming pools. So far, 3 people have tested positive for cryptosporidium and 8 others are under investigation after a regular attendance at the farm.\n\nDoctors in the Gwent area have been told to alert people about the disease. Heather Lewis, a consultant in health protection for Public Health Wales, said: '[Cryptosporidiosis] is a disease that is very common in young farm animals and can easily be passed to people who come into contact with those animals.\n\n'All the confirmed cases had direct contact with the lambs at the college farm. As part of our investigations, we are checking on all those whom we believe had contact with these animals and Coleg Gwent is co\u00adoperating fully with our investigations.\n\n_Cryptosporidium_ is a single celled parasite that can cause a disease called cryptosporidiosis. Both humans and animals can catch the illness. It is the biggest cause of human gastrointestinal infection in Britain. The symptoms include diarrhoea, vomiting, stomach ache, and weight loss. Ms Lewis said: '[Cryptosporidiosis] is a disease that is very common in young farm animals and can easily be passed to people who come into contact with those animals. 'Good hand washing after coming into contact with farm animals is of the utmost importance in preventing infection with cryptosporidium,' Ms Lewis said. 'There is no reason for anyone to avoid visiting petting farms as long as they ensure that anyone who has touched animals, thoroughly washes their hands with hot water and soap immediately afterwards.'\n\n[Byline: Emma James]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[_Cryptosporidium_ spp. is endemic in farm animals, especially calves, which is also an important source of contamination of surface water. Canada: (QC) 1st rep", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=4192359", "date": "2016-04-29 21:00:13", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - CANADA: (QUEBEC) FIRST REPORT\n*************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Thu 28 Apr 2016\nSource: CTV News [edited]\nhttp://www.ctvnews.ca/health/tropical-parasite-emerges-in-indigenous-communities-in-far-north-1.2878669\n\n\nCanadian researchers have discovered an intestinal parasite known as _Cryptosporidium_ for the 1st time in a remote community in the Arctic.\n\nThe potentially dangerous parasite was found in Nunavik, in Quebec's far north.\n\nA team from the Research Institute of the McGill University Health Centre [MUHC] made the find after examining an outbreak of intestinal illnesses in 10 Indigenous villages between April 2013 and April 2014.\n\nThe researchers were able to identify the parasite as _Cryptosporidium hominis_, a strain of the protozoa that is normally found in tropical countries. _Cryptosporidium_ lives in the intestine of mammals, including humans, and is typically spread by drinking water contaminated with feces.\n\nThe resulting illness, called cryptosporidiosis or simply 'crypto', causes diarrhea, cramps, and vomiting that can last for weeks and that can be fatal to young children and those with weakened immune systems.\n\nThe discovery is worrisome, says the study's senior author, Dr C\u00e9dric Yansouni, who is associate director of the JD MacLean Centre for Tropical Diseases at MUHC. That's because _Cryptosporidium_ illness could have long-term implications on the health of children in Nunavik as well as in nearby Nunavut.\n\n'We are being particularly vigilant because it is known in low-income countries that repeated _Cryptosporidium_ infections can cause growth delays and difficulty at school in children,' he said in a statement.\n\n'In the Nunavik outbreak, children under the age of 5 were the group most affected by the infection.'\n\nThere is a treatment for cryptosporidiosis that is readily available in countries where _Cryptosporidium_ is common, but it is only available in Canada under a special access program.\n\n_Cryptosporidium_ outbreaks are rare in most of Canada. In 2001, more than 7000 residents of North Battleford, Saskatchewan became sick when _Cryptosporidium_ entered the city's water supply during routine maintenance of a chemical filter.\n\nIn 1997, there was an outbreak in Shoal Lake 40 First Nation, which straddles the Ontario-Manitoba boundary. The community has been under a boil-water advisory ever since.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[It must be a mistake that _Cryptosporidium_ spp. is labeled as a tropical parasite. 'Diarrhoea and tummy cramps for 2 weeks aren't much fun.'\n\n'Some of the cases were children but half were adults, and similar outbreaks have also been seen in other parts of New Zealand. 'The main symptoms are diarrhoea, often watery, stomach cramps, lack of appetite and weight loss,' Dr Jarman said. The illness may last 2 weeks or more. There is no treatment except for making sure that the patient does not become dehydrated.\n\nThe illness is caused by a tiny parasite found in the guts of birds, fish, reptiles (such as geckos and turtles), humans and animals such as cattle, sheep, cats and dogs. It is passed on in the faeces of infected animals and humans.\n\nThe usual risk factors for cryptosporidiosis are living on a farm and having contact with farm animals. 'However, some cases in Taranaki became unwell after recently swimming in a pool,' Dr Jarman said. 'It is important that people with recent tummy bug symptoms stay away from swimming pools, otherwise they may spread the illness to others,' he said.\n\nThe bugs are excreted by sick people for several weeks once they have recovered from the illness. People should not go swimming in a pool if they have diarrhoea. Wait until at least 2 weeks after the symptoms have gone. See a doctor if the symptoms are severe, if worried or if the illness does not get better after 2-3 days.\n\nWash hands thoroughly after contact with animals or animal faeces, after caring for people with diarrhoea, after going to the toilet, and before and during food preparation. The parasite cyst is passed on to humans through contact with animals, contaminated water or food, or through direct contact with human waste such as when changing nappies.\n\nThe disease usually causes diarrhoea, but children may go off their food and vomit before the diarrhoea starts. Diarrhoea is often watery and tends to be accompanied by stomach cramps. Symptoms can come and go but have usually stopped within 4 weeks. In rare cases when a person's immune system is very weak the disease can go on longer and cause complications.\n\nHand hygiene is the key to preventing spread in households and is particularly important for parents with sick children in nappies. Parents should keep young children away from day care for at least 48 hours after symptoms clear. Anyone who has symptoms and handles food at work should also stay off work for 48 hours after symptoms clear.\n\nAnyone with symptoms should stay out of swimming pools for at least 2 weeks after symptoms disappear. This is because people who have had the illness can continue to excrete cysts that are resistant to chlorine. Larger public pools generally have systems for filtering out crypto cysts, but in pools without microfiltration crypto may survive for weeks.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Kunihiko Iizuka\n\n[Cryptosporidiosis is well known in many parts of the world. which can cause watery diarrhoea, stomach pain, nausea or vomiting and fever - with cases having been reported since mid-August [2012].\n\n7 of those affected had used the pool, and after water sampling revealed the presence of the bug _Cryptosporidium_, it was closed last Thursday [7 Sep 2012].\nInvestigations are continuing but it is not known how the other 7 people were infected.\n'We know that 7 of the 14 people who have become unwell since mid-August have visited the swimming pool at the Newport Centre,' said Dr Lika Nehaul, consultant in communicable disease control for Public Health Wales.\n\nHowever, some had other possible exposure that could explain their infection, so we are keeping an open mind about any possible links. The pool remains closed and no date has been set for its reopening.\n\nCryptosporidiosis can affect people of any age but is most common in children aged\n1-5 years. It is often associated with travel overseas and can be acquired from other people, from animals [cattle], and from drinking or swimming in contaminated water.\n\nAlthough outbreaks of cryptosporidiosis do occur, it is not unusual for Public Health Wales to be notified of sporadic cases throughout the year, that cannot be linked to others. There are up to 400 cases a year in Wales.\n\n'Stay away from work if you have symptoms'\n------------------------------------------\n'Anyone with symptoms should stay away from work or school until 48 hours after symptoms have stopped,' said Dr Nehaul. CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - USA (04): (IDAHO) SWIMMING POOLS\n****************************************************\n The parasite _Cryptosporidium_ can live in human and animal [cattle] digestive tracts and can spread through swimming pools and other recreational water sites that are contaminated with human or animal waste. EP]\n\n[_Cryptosporidium_ life cycle diagram:\nhttp://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Cryptosporidiosis_01.png/300px-Cryptosporidiosis_01.png - Mod.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "cryptosporidiosis"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-04 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Idaho", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "USA", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Gooding", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1600837", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Cryptosporidiosis - UK: food borne", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1600837", "date": "2013-03-24 11:20:41", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - UK: FOOD BORNE\n**********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Wed 20 Mar 2013\nSource: Telegraph UK [edited]\nhttp://www.telegraph.co.uk/health/healthnews/9939839/Ready-to-eat-salads-from-Morrisons-and-Asda-caused-infection-outbreak-HPA-say.html\n\n\n'Ready-to-eat' salads from 2 major supermarket chains were behind a stomach infection which poisoned 300 shoppers, the Health Protection Agency has claimed.\n\nCryptosporidia have been traced back to pre-cut bagged salad products which are likely to have been labelled as 'ready-to-eat,' the HPA said. The salads are believed to have been bought from Morrisons and Asda, although an HPA spokeswoman pointed out that most supermarket chains in the UK have the same suppliers, so any one of them could have been affected.\n\nA sample of 25 percent of those who became unwell found 46 percent recalled eating mixed-leaf bagged salad from Morrisons, while 11 percent ate spinach from the Asda chain, the HPA reported. The recall rate is 'extremely high,' as people often cannot remember what they bought, and the figures provide a 'statistically significant association,' the agency say.\n\nExperts believe that the cryptosporidium infection may have come from the spinach, which would have been in both bags. But Morrisons denied that claim, saying the HPA seemed to want to make an 'eye catching announcement' before they are disbanded in 2 weeks.\n\nThe infection is caused by a parasite [_Cryptosporidium_] in the intestine, which causes an acute, short term infection, the most common symptom of which is diarrhoea. The pre-packed salads have come under criticism before, with scientists claiming that they would lead to an increase in food poisoning.\n\nAfter the cryptosporidium outbreak in England and Scotland in May 2012, the HPA launched an investigation. The outbreak was short lived, with most experiencing only moderate symptoms, and the numbers of cases returned to expected seasonal levels within a month of the 1st cases being reported. When they interviewed the people who became unwell about their food history, the HPA discovered the link between the illness and the salads sold at the 2 supermarkets.\n\nThis is not the 1st time pre-packaged vegetables have been linked to illness. A salmonella outbreak in the UK in 2007 was traced back to imported basil, while an _E. coli_ outbreak in America in 2006 was linked back to pre-packed baby spinach. A link to spinach from retailers other than Morrisons and Asda was also suggested but was found to be inconclusive. A spokesperson said: 'Together, these findings suggest that one or more types of salad vegetables could have been contaminated.'\n\nThe HPA confirmed that they could not identify contaminated products in any particular chain of supermarkets because of the time lapse, but interviewing a sample of those who fell ill was a investigation method accepted by health organisations across the world.\n\nThe Food Standards Agency also gathered information on the production and distribution of salad vegetables to try to identify the likely source of the outbreak. But despite investigating the food chain, including the practice and procedures throughout each stage of growing, processing, packing and distribution, they have not managed to identify a source of contamination.\n\nBagged salad on sale in supermarkets is often sourced from the same suppliers for most leaf types, often with common production lines packing product for several retailers at the same time. This was the situation in this case. Dr Stephen Morton, regional director of the HPA's Yorkshire and the Humber region and head of the multi-agency Outbreak Control Team, said: 'This outbreak was fortunately short lived, but it was important to see if we could find the source. Our findings suggest that eating mixed leaf bagged salad was the most likely cause of illness. It is, however, often difficult to identify the source of short lived outbreaks of this type, as by the time that the outbreak can be investigated, the affected food and much of the microbiological evidence may no longer be available.'\n\n'As this was an isolated and short lived outbreak, there is no specific action for the public to take, but we hope the investigations between the FSA and the food industry will help to prevent further outbreaks of this type from happening again.' The FSA added: 'This would appear to have been an isolated, short-lived outbreak, and it does not appear that there are any on-going problems.' They said that consumers should continue to have 'confidence' in ready to eat products.\n\nDr Alison Gleadle, director of food safety at the FSA, reminded people to keep their kitchens clean and wash non-ready-to-eat vegetables. Morrisons say that there is no conclusive evidence for a link between their salad and the outbreak. A spokesperson said: 'Morrisons is not the source of this outbreak. We have received no complaints of illness, and no Morrisons products have tested positive for cryptosporidia. The HPA's claim is based solely on statistics, not testing. The very same statistics also implicated products from other retailers that the HPA recognise as 'implausible.' ... 'The HPA appears to be concerned with making an eye catching announcement before being disbanded in 2 weeks time.'\n\nThere are a number of potential sources for the cryptosporidium parasite, including consumption of contaminated water or food, swimming in contaminated water, or through contact with contaminated food or affected animals. Asda have also denied links to the virus outbreak, claiming that investigation by the HPA is 'statistically flawed.' A spokesperson added: 'Product safety is our top priority, and if we had any serious concerns, we'd act immediately. So far, this has not been necessary.'\n\n[Byline: Hayley Dixon]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Food processing and manufacture are increasingly centralized and distributed to many shops. This increases the risk of infecting many people at different locations. 'In another circumstance they might not have a clear idea of who all was there,' she said Thursday.\n\nAll on the list have been contacted. If they have or had symptoms -- diarrhea, stomach cramps, vomiting, loss of appetite, weight loss and low-grade fever -- they were asked to follow up with their health care provider. Source: Republican Eagle [edited]\nhttp://www.republican-eagle.com/event/article/id/80747/\n\n\nCryptosporidiosis after attending petting zoo\n \n\nThe Minnesota Department of Health issued an alert to health care providers Wednesday afternoon after laboratory tests confirmed 2 people picked up the parasite, which causes severe watery diarrhea.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "cryptosporidiosis"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-04-21 00:00:00", "2021-03-24 00:00:00", "2021-03-02 00:00:00", "2021-03-31 00:00:00", "2021-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Goodhue County", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "1239421", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Cryptosporidiosis - USA (03): (ID)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=1239421", "date": "2012-08-11 09:28:56", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - USA (03): (IDAHO)\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Fri, 10 Aug 2012\nSource: Idaho Statesman [edited]\nhttp://www.idahostatesman.com/2012/08/09/2225121/crypto-outbreak-in-boise-meridian.html\n\nCrypto outbreak in Boise and Meridian, Idaho\n--------------------------------------------\nThe past 10 days the Central District Health Department has seen 19 cases of cryptosporidiosis. During a normal year the health district might see 10 cases.\n\nCrypto [Cryptosporidium] is a parasite that can cause diarrhea, abdominal pain, vomiting and fever. It can spread through swimming pools and other recreational water. Crypto cases have been reported in Boise and Meridian, but the disease appears to be widespread in the community.\n\nAs a result of the outbreak, the health department is urging the public to take precautions to prevent the spread of the disease.\n\nThe health department has advised area swimming pool operators of the situation and many responded by hyper-chlorinating the pools, a technique that kills the parasite. Still, those efforts can be rendered ineffective when people carrying the disease use recreational waters.\n\n'We know the hot weather is driving people to seek relief in area pools, lakes and rivers,' Kimberly Link, Program Manager for Communicable Disease Control at CDHD, said in a press release. 'If you've been ill with diarrhea we can't emphasize enough how important it is to stay out of recreational waters for at least two weeks after your symptoms resolve.'\n\nCryptosporidiosis, commonly known as crypto, is usually spread by accidentally swallowing water that has been contaminated with human stool or animal waste. The most frequent symptom is watery diarrhea with abdominal cramping, which can be accompanied by dehydration, weight loss, abdominal pain, fever, nausea, and vomiting. If people have been in recreational waters and are experiencing persistent, watery diarrhea they are urged to consult their healthcare provider.\n\nOnce a person is infected, the parasites live in the intestines and are passed in the stool. In most instances, infected persons recover without medication. However, a healthcare provider may choose to use medication in cases that do not improve or when other medical issues exist. Infected persons can continue to spread the disease up to two weeks after diarrhea subsides, so they should avoid activities involving recreational waters.\n\nBecause crypto can survive for days even in properly chlorinated pools, the health department is urging people to follow these six steps to help reduce the spread of recreational water illnesses:\n\n1. Don't swim when you have diarrhea. This is especially important for kids in diapers. If you have been diagnosed with crypto, please stay out of swimming pools and recreational waters for at least two weeks after symptoms are gone.\n2. Don't swallow the pool water. In fact, avoid getting water in your mouth.\n3. Practice good hygiene. Take a shower before swimming and wash your hands after using the toilet or changing diapers. Germs on your body end up in the water.\n4. Take your kids on regular bathroom breaks or change diapers often, and use swim diapers. Waiting to hear 'I have to go' may mean that it's too late.\n5. Change diapers in a bathroom; not at the poolside. Germs can spread to surfaces around the pool and spread illness.\n6. Wash your child thoroughly (especially the rear end) with soap and water before swimming. Everyone has invisible amounts of fecal matter on their bottoms that end up in the pool.Day care providers are urged not to take children with diarrhea to swimming pools. Pool users are also reminded to wash their hands with soap and water\nbefore eating or snacking at poolside.\n\nFor additional information about cryptosporidiosis, please visit the CDC's website at: http://www.cdc.gov/parasites/crypto.\n\n--\nCommunicated by ProMED-mail from Health map alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Transmission of cryptosporidia in swimming polls is well known. As pointed out in the press release above, Cryptosporidia are resistant to chlorine in standard concentrations.\nProMED stopped reporting small outbreaks of Crytosporidiosis in the USA in 2005 as the infection is endemic, which explain the small number of reports after 2005. - Mod. EP\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/r/1CnG.]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n \n\nBecause crypto can survive for days even in properly chlorinated pools, the health department is urging people to follow these six steps to help reduce the spread of recreational water illnesses:\n\n1. If you've been ill with diarrhea we can't emphasize enough how important it is to stay out of recreational waters for at least two weeks after your symptoms resolve.'\n\nCryptosporidiosis, commonly known as crypto, is usually spread by accidentally swallowing water that has been contaminated with human stool or animal waste. Pool users are also reminded to wash their hands with soap and water\nbefore eating or snacking at poolside. CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - USA (03): (IDAHO)\n********************************\n For additional information about cryptosporidiosis, please visit the CDC's website at: http://www.cdc.gov/parasites/crypto.\n\n--\n http://www.idahostatesman.com/2012/08/09/2225121/crypto-outbreak-in-boise-meridian.html\n\nCrypto outbreak in Boise and Meridian, Idaho\n If you have been diagnosed with crypto, please stay out of swimming pools and recreational waters for at least two weeks after symptoms are gone. It can spread through swimming pools and other recreational water. \nProMED stopped reporting small outbreaks of Crytosporidiosis in the USA in 2005 as the infection is endemic, which explain the small number of reports after 2005. If people have been in recreational waters and are experiencing persistent, watery diarrhea they are urged to consult their healthcare provider. The most frequent symptom is watery diarrhea with abdominal cramping, which can be accompanied by dehydration, weight loss, abdominal pain, fever, nausea, and vomiting. The parasite Cryptosporidium is normally associated with contained swimming pools, lakes and salad foods but no source has yet been identified in this outbreak.\n\nPeople in the North East, Yorkshire, West and East Midlands have been\ninfected. Usually outbreaks remain isolated and the source of the infection is quickly identified.\n\nThe Health Protection Agency has investigated the drinking water supply and\nit is thought this is an unlikely cause of the outbreak. The organism can live in soil, food, water and on surfaces contaminated by human or animal faeces.\n\nThe infection known as cryptosporidiosis causes diarrhoea, stomach pains,\ndehydration, weight loss and fever which can least a month or more. Symptoms\ncan be severe in the very young and the elderly, however most people\naffected in the outbreak so far are adults aged between 15 and 45.\n\nA 'handful' of people have been admitted to hospital and have now recovered,\na spokesman for the HPA said. Dr Stephen Morton, who is leading the investigation for the HPA, said: 'It is usual to see an increase in cryptosporidiosis cases in the early summer, but, the increase is higher than we might expect so we are working with NHS partners, local Environmental Health Officers the Drinking Water Inspectorate and the Food Standards Agency to see if there is a common source of infection.\n\n'GPs in affected areas have been informed about the recent rise in cases and\nasked to be vigilant to further possible cases. Minnesota Department of Health (MDH) epidemiologist Trisha Robinson said the confirmed cases probably only represent a fraction of people who were actually sickened by the parasite. The investigation of the outbreak is still in progress.\n\n'One different thing with cryptosporidiosis is the time from when a person is exposed to the time when they become sick can be as long as 2 weeks,' Robinson said. 'Pools were closed on 26 March, so we could still have people becoming ill 2 weeks after that, and their symptoms last anywhere from one to 2 weeks.'\n\nAnother unrelated cryptosporidiosis outbreak in Brainerd [Minnesota] last month resulted in 36 reported cases, with one case being confirmed in a laboratory.\n\nThe symptoms of cryptosporidiosis include stomach cramps, fevers or diarrhea. It can be contracted by swimming in infected water, contact with animals or even drinking raw unpasteurized milk.\n\n'The way that cryptosporidiosis is typically introduced into the water park,and which we believe is the instance in both of these cases, is that it comes in from an infected user,' Robinson said.\n\nIn 2011, there were 305 laboratory-confirmed cases of cryptosporidiosis, according to preliminary MDH numbers. Robinson said one person was hospitalized in each of the Brainerd and Duluth outbreaks.\n\n'It certainly can be more dangerous to those who are immune-compromised: the children, the very elderly, pregnant individuals,' Robinson said. 'In Minnesota, we can see about 20 percent of individuals that would require hospitalization for this, so it is something that can cause very serious illness.' Robinson said that people who have been sick with diarrhea in the previous 2 weeks should avoid swimming in recreational waters.\n\n--\nCommunicated by\nPromed Mail from Health Map alerts\n\n[Cryptosporidiosis is endemic in the United States and outbreaks in water parks have been reported before, although not as frequently as outbreaks in swimming pools. Fearing an outbreak of the nasty\ngastro bug, which 10 years ago left more than 1000 people ill after\nswimming, health officials yesterday [27 Feb 2009] warned the public\nto be cautious.\n\nAt least 19 pools have been ordered to super-chlorinate their water\nafter infected people identified they became sick after swimming, The\nDaily Telegraph reports. The germ spreads with such ferocity,\nespecially in summer and in pools, that 44 cases alone have been\nidentified in the past day.\n\nNSW Health's director of communicable diseases Dr Jeremy McAnulty\nsaid anyone who had diarrhoea in the past fortnight needed to stay\nout of pools.\n\n'While there is no common link among most cases, some have reported\nswimming in common pools,' he said. 'Pools can be easily contaminated\nby infectious swimmers, and so it is vital that people take care.'\n\nNSW Health said there was no evidence these pools were the cause of\nany infection. Most of the affected pools are from western Sydney.\nNSW Health is refusing to name them individually, fearing it will lay\nblame and cause further panic. All pools associated with the bug have\nbeen tested, but no evidence of contamination has been found.\n\nCryptosporidiosis is a diarrhoeal disease caused by a parasitic\ninfection of the intestine. It surges in summer, usually because\npeople are outdoors drinking contaminated water and playing with\nanimals. Children are most susceptible with half of the confirmed\ncases under 5.\n\nDr McAnulty said the number of cases had doubled since January 2009,\nsparking concern it could soon mirror the horror outbreak of 1998.\n'We don't know if any pool is the source of the outbreak, but we know\nfrom previous outbreaks that swimming is a link,' he said.\n\n'Cryptosporidiosis is a bug that lives in animals, and people can\ncarry it. Once it gets into swimming pools, then it can really\namplify.' The last major outbreak was the summer of 1998. The same\nyear, panic gripped Sydney when bugs, giardia and cryptosporidiosis\nwere found in the Prospect water filtration plant. It became known as\nSydney's water crisis, and households were ordered to boil water.\n\nDespite the fear that thousands of people would fall ill, not one\nperson was found to become sick.\n\nThere is no treatment for the infection. Symptoms include diarrhoea,\nstomach cramps, fever, nausea and vomiting, which can last weeks and\nsometimes even months. Some persons do not present any symptoms. If\nsymptoms are present, they often last about 2 weeks, and then you may\nfeel better.\n\n[Byline: Kate Sikora]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[ProMED's last report of crytosporidiosis from New South Wales\n(Cryptosporidiosis - Australia (SA) (02): comments 20070324.1029)\npoints out that person-to-person transmission is more common than\nexpected.\n\nCryptosporidiosis is endemic in New South Wales, and surveillance is\nwell developed (Viney KA and McAnulty JM. The outbreak has severely\naffected homes and businesses in Ireland's 3rd-biggest city, and\nresidents have been warned that the crisis could last for months.\nDoctors have warned that the parasite could be life threatening,\nespecially to young children, the elderly and people with low immune systems.\nTests found that the city's water supply contained nearly 60 times\nthe safe limit of cryptosporidium pollution.\nResidents have already been unable to drink or use water for food\npreparation for weeks and have complained that no free clean water\nhas been made available by the authorities.\nParents with young families have also expressed real concerns and\ndemanded action. The mayor of Galway, Niall O Brolchain, has called on the\nIrish government to provide more funding to upgrade water treatment\nservices. 'If you've got 51 cases confirmed in a lab, chances are there are hundreds out there,' Public Health medical officer Dr Steven Donohue said.\n\nCairns and Hinterland Health District recorded 51 lab-tested cases of crypto in January [2012], Dr Donohue said. 'In a normal year you might get 10 or 20 cases,' he said, adding the last outbreak in Cairns was in 2008. Dr Donohue sent a warning letter to day care centres to alert them to the outbreak of crypto.\n\nIn the letter, Dr Donohue instructed day care centres to exclude children with diarrhoea until they have not had symptoms for 48 hours. He also recommended that swimming pools at day care centres be disinfected with adequate chlorination or refilled after each session.\n\nQueensland Health is also in the process of notifying swimming pool operators about the health risk, Dr Donohue said. 'We're not blaming the pools but they are a known factor in magnifying the outbreak,' he said. 'The pool operators should be very careful to make sure children with diarrhoea or dirty nappies are not in pools.' Dr Donohue said children who have had diarrhoea should not swim in a public pool for at least 2 weeks after symptoms stop. Cairns Regional Council pools are tested regularly and would continue to be monitored closely, a spokeswoman said.\n\nIn May 2010, the parasite that causes crypto was detected in southern Cairns' water supply, prompting warnings at that time for thousands of residents to boil their drinking water. A council spokeswoman yesterday said the drinking water supply is regularly tested and there was no sign of the parasite that causes crypto in the water.\n\nWhile crypto is generally not serious for most people, those with weakened immune systems could develop severe and long-lasting illness, which may contribute to death. The best protection from crypto is thorough hand washing before eating and after using the toilet, Dr Donohue said.\n\n[Byline: Tarina White]\n\n[Promed Mail from\nHealth map alerts]\n\n[The 2009 outbreak in Sydney (Mayne DJ et al. A community outbreak of cryptosporidiosis in Sydney associated with a public swimming facility: a case-control study. Interdiscip Perspect Infect Dis. 2011;2011:341065) was found to be due to infection at swimming pools and was caused by _Cryptosporidium hominis_ subtype IbA10G2 [see ProMED ref. below].\n\nCryptosporidia in man and animals in Australia have recently been reviewed:\nWaldron LS et al. Molecular epidemiology, spatiotemporal analysis, and ecology of sporadic human cryptosporidiosis in Australia. Appl Environ Microbiol. 2011;77:7757-65).\n\nHealthMap location: http://healthmap.org/r/1Km3 - Mod. A council spokeswoman yesterday said the drinking water supply is regularly tested and there was no sign of the parasite that causes crypto in the water. \n\nQueensland Health is also in the process of notifying swimming pool operators about the health risk, Dr Donohue said. \n\nWhile crypto is generally not serious for most people, those with weakened immune systems could develop severe and long-lasting illness, which may contribute to death. In May 2010, the parasite that causes crypto was detected in southern Cairns' water supply, prompting warnings at that time for thousands of residents to boil their drinking water. The finding means it\nis unlikely that an infected animal or environmental conditions, such\nas the weather, are to blame. Since the beginning of last month\n[February 2009], when the latest outbreak appeared, 628 people have\nfallen ill, compared with 482 in all of last year [2008].\n\nMacquarie University researchers say their testing is experimental,\nand unlikely to identify the origin of the current outbreak, but they\nhope their work will lead to the development of a world-leading,\ninexpensive automated test to quickly identify parasite strains\ninvolved in future outbreaks, allowing sources to be rapidly traced\nand infections contained.\n\nMichelle Power, of Macquarie's biological sciences department, said\ncryptosporidium outbreaks were difficult to trace using existing\ntechnology. There are 2 key parasite 'species' that infect people:\n_Cryptosporidium hominis_, only contracted by direct human to human\ncontact, and _Cryptosporidium parvum_, which can also be transmitted by\nanimals. For each type there were about 40 different strains.\n\nHowever, virtually nothing was known about which strains were\nresponsible for most outbreaks. A decade after the contamination of\nWarragamba Dam, which forced Sydneysiders to boil their water, 'we\nstill just know that it was cryptosporidium,' Dr Power said. 'To put\nit simply, we're looking for the easiest way to fingerprint the organisms.'\n\nWith the new test it should be possible to compare results from\nindividual patients to seek common patterns.\n\n'We could look to see if they had all eaten at the same restaurant,\nor been to the same day-care centre or swimming pool.'\n\nThe NSW Health Department has provided hundreds of fecal samples from\nthe latest outbreak for testing by a Macquarie University parasite\nresearcher, Liette Waldron.\n\n'We have analysed about 250 samples from the current outbreak, and\nthey are all the same strain' of _Cryptosporidium hominis_, Dr Power said.\n\nIf it had originated from different sources she would have expected\nto see multiple strains. When sporadic cases, reported over the past\n12 months, were analysed they identified 24 strains, involving both\nthe human and animal species.\n\nDr Power said people who were infected, but not necessarily appearing\nill, usually had 5000 to 10 000 parasites per gram of faeces.\nHowever, some samples from the latest outbreak contained more than 1\nmillion. 'The more parasites you see, the more nasty it is.'\n\nOver the next 2 years the project, funded by the Australian Research\nCouncil and the Health Department,will 'fingerprint' parasites from\nup to 4000 cryptosporidium patients.\n\n[Byline: Richard Macey]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Genetic fingerprinting is a very useful tool to identify the spread\nof microorganisms in a community and thus to help identify the source\nand transmissison routes. The new knowledge that the outbreak\nprobably has a single human source underlines the importance of\ninterrupting transmission to control the outbreak. - Mod.EP\n\nLife-cycle diagram of _Cryptosporidium hominis_ at:\n<http://www.dpd.cdc.gov/DPDx/images/ParasiteImages/A-F/Cryptosporidiosis/Cryptosporidium_LifeCycle.gif>\nElectron micrograph of _C. parvum_ at:\n<http://www.qualitywater.co.uk/images/clip_image001.jpg>\n- Mod.JW\n\nA map showing the location of New South Wales in Australia can be found at:\n<http://www.infoplease.com/atlas/country/australia.html>.\n- CopyEd.EJP]", "summary": "\n\nMacquarie University researchers say their testing is experimental,\nand unlikely to identify the origin of the current outbreak, but they\nhope their work will lead to the development of a world-leading,\ninexpensive automated test to quickly identify parasite strains\ninvolved in future outbreaks, allowing sources to be rapidly traced\nand infections contained. [1,2] (primary references available on request):\n\nRates of cryptosporidiosis in Australia are somewhat higher than those in New Zealand, and 7- to 8-fold those reported in the United States -- graph follows [3] http://www.gideononline.com/wp/wp-content/uploads/Cryptosporid-ANZ.png\n\nInfecting species\n-----------------\nSporadic cases of cryptosporidiosis are caused by _Cryptosporidium hominis_, _C. parvum_, _C. andersoni_, and _C. fayeri_.\n_Cryptosporidium parvum_ has been detected in the Sydney water supply since 1998.\n_Cryptosporidium canis_ has been recovered from dogs, and _C. felis_ from cats -- neither posing a significant public health risk to humans.\n_Cryptosporidium_ sp. have been recovered from urban-adapted Common brushtail possums (_Trichosurus vulpecula_) and barramundi (_Lates calcarifer_)\n_Cryptosporidium cuniculus_ has been identified in rabbits.\n_Cryptosporidium fayeri_, a species associated with marsupials, has been identified in a case of human infection.\n\nPrevalence surveys\n------------------\n- 2.2 percent of HIV-positive MSM [men who have sex with men], 0.6 percent of HIV-negative MSM, and 3 percent of controls (Sydney, 2003 to 2006)\n- 0.8 percent of stool samples from symptomatic and asymptomatic subjects (Sydney, 2008 to 2009)\n- 24.5 percent of pre-weaned sheep (Western Australia, 2009 publication)\n- 22.3 percent of pre-weaned calves (Western Australia and New South Wales, 2010 publication)\n- 58.5 percent of dairy and beef calves with diarrhea (_Cryptosporidium_ spp., 2011 publication)\n- 8.5 percent of fecal samples from animals living in Sydney water catchments (_Cryptosporidium_ spp., 2011 publication)\n- 71.4 to 81.3 percent of fecal samples from lambs in southern Western Australia (2011 publication)\n- 9.3 percent of western grey kangaroos (_Macropus fuliginosus_) in Western Australia -- 25 percent of these identified as _Cryptosporidium fayeri_ (2011 publication)\n\nNotable outbreaks\n-----------------\n- 1991 (publication year): an outbreak of cryptosporidiosis was reported among children in Adelaide.\n- 1994 to 1995: outbreaks (70 cases) were reported in the Sutherland area of Sydney during September 1994 to January 1995 -- 17 of these acquired from a swimming pool.\n- 1998: an outbreak (161 cases confirmed) was reported in The Australian Capital Territory -- 60 percent related to 2 public swimming pools.\n- 1998: outbreaks related to swimming pools was reported in Brisbane, Sydney, and Melbourne.\n- 2001: an outbreak (47 cases) in Tasmania was ascribed to animal contact at an agricultural show.\n- 2002 (publication year): An outbreak was associated with drinking unpasteurized milk.\n- 2005: outbreaks (180 cases, approximate) were associated with swimming pools in New South Wales.\n- 2008: an outbreak in Sydney was associated with a swimming pool.\n- 2009: an outbreak (1141 cases) was associated with swimming pools in New South Wales.\n- 2009: an outbreak (628 cases during January to March) was reported in Sydney.\n\nReferences\n----------\n1. Berger SA: Infectious Diseases of Australia, 2012. 515 pp, 160 graphs, 2930 references. Gideon e-books, http://www.gideononline.com/ebooks/country/infectious-diseases-of-australia/\n2. Berger SA: Cryptosporidiosis: Global Status, 2012. 77 pp, 47 graphs, 995 references. Gideon e-books, http://www.gideononline.com/ebooks/disease/cryptosporidiosis-global-status/\n3. See Gideon graph tool at http://www.GIDEONonline.com/wp/wp-content/uploads/Gideon-Graphs.pps\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nDr Steve Berger\nGeographic Medicine\nTel Aviv Medical Center\nIsrael\n<mberger@post.tau.ac.il>\n\n******\n[2]\nDate: Sun 5 Feb 2012\nFrom: Stanley Cassan <scassan@sbcglobal.net> [edited]\n\n\nJust a comment concerning the outbreak of cryptosporidiosis in Cairns, Australia reported 5 Feb volume 2012, number 066. Dr Donohoe has advised adequate swimming pool chlorination as a preventive measure.\n\nHowever the parasite is chlorine resistant and other measures such as ozonation or ultaviolet treatment of the water would be needed to reduce infectivity. Certainly, changing the swimming pool water after each use as he suggests would be helpful.\n\n--\nStanley M Cassan MD\nCarmel, CA\nUSA\n<scassan@sbcglobal.net>\n\n[ProMED-mail thanks Steve Berger for providing background information and Stanley M Cassan for pointing out that _Cryptosporidium_ is inherently resistant to chlorination.\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of Queensland can be seen at http://healthmap.org/r/1Km3. - The analysis\nfound cryptosporidium in both drinking water\nsamples and samples obtained from the purification unit.\n\nIt is, therefore, highly likely that\ncryptosporidium in the municipal water supply has\nbeen the cause of the many cases of diarrhoeal\ndisease seen in Ostersund during the last few\nweeks. Further samples are being analyzed at SMI,\nand the results will be ready by 1 or 2 Dec 2010.\n\nThe water purification unit at Missesgarde\nobtains its water from Lake Storsjon and supplies\ndrinking water to the municipalities of\n\u00d6stersund, Brunflo, Lockne, Froson and the area\nfrom Genvalla to Orrviken, and the areas of\nByskogen and Sanghusvallen in Krokom municipality.\n\nOstersund municipality, Jamtland Regional\nGovernment, and SMI are working to identify the\nsource of infection and, in the meantime,\nrecommend that people living in the areas listed\nabove continue to boil their drinking water until further notice.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\n\n******\n[2]\nDate: Mon 29 Nov 2010\nSource: BeforeItsNews.com [edited]\n<http://beforeitsnews.com/story/285/919/Cryptosporidium_outbreak_sickens_thousands_in_Sweden.html>\n\n\nUPI reports that cryptosporidium in the water\nsupply has sickened more than 2000 people in the\ncity of Ostersund, in northern Sweden. See\ngraph at:\n<http://exhibit.gideononline.com/Cry=ptosporid-Ireland.jpg>\n(U.S. rates added for comparison).\nCryptosporidiosis became a notifiable disease in\nIreland in January 2004, with 61 percent of cases\nhaving been reported during April to June\n[?2004]. A total of 5 outbreaks (25 outbreak\ncases) was reported in 2004. A total of 6\noutbreaks (49 outbreak cases) was reported in 2005.\nPrevalence surveys:\n4 percent of gastroenteritis among children.\n4 percent of diarrhea requiring hospitalization among Dublin Children 1987.\n149 cases were reported among children below age 2 years in 2002; 106 in 2003.\n64 patients were hospitalized for\ncryptosporidiosis in 1999; 57 in 2000; 65 in\n2001; 53 in 2002. 70 cases (18 per 100 000) were\nreported by the South-Eastern Health Board in\n2001. 66 cases (19 per 100 000) were reported by\nthe Western Health Board in 2001.\nInfected local shellfish species:\n_Mytilus edulis_ (Common mussel) in the Sligo Area\n_Dreissena polymorpha_ (Zebra mussel) in the Shannon River drainage area.\nNotable outbreaks:\n2002 - An outbreak (125 cases) was reported in Galway.\nNorthern Ireland:\nNotable outbreaks:\n2000 - An outbreak (121 confirmed cases) was\nreported - with most cases in the County Antrim\nand County Down towns of Lisburn and Bangor.\n2001 - USA: (MN)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=566679", "date": "2010-10-30 13:00:04", "main_text": "CAMPYLOBACTERIOSIS, CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS, RAW MILK - USA: (MINNESOTA)\n****************************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Thu 28 Oct 2010\nSource: Associated Press [edited]\n<http://www.businessweek.com/ap/financialnews/D9J4UMJO0.htm>\n\n\nIllnesses in 7 more people have been tied by to the consumption of\nraw milk from a farm in Sibley County that was implicated in an _E.\ncoli_ [O157:H7] outbreak earlier in 2010, the Minnesota Department of\nHealth reported Thursday [28 Oct 2010]. 3 people were infected with a\nbacterium called _Campylobacter jejuni_ and the other 4 with a\nparasite, _Cryptosporidium parvum_, after drinking raw milk. All 7\nreported drinking raw milk, and those who named a source said it was\nthe Hartmann Dairy Farm, the department said.\n\nLaboratory tests determined the bacterium and parasite in most of the\nill people were genetically identical to samples taken from the farm\nthis summer. Both result in similar symptoms, including fever,\ndiarrhea and vomiting. Cryptosporidiosis can be life threatening for\npeople with weak immune systems.\n\n'We know all these things are carried by cattle and transmitted by\nraw milk. It's not unexpected,' said Kirk Smith, a foodborne diseases\nexpert with the health department. 'Whatever is in cow poop is going\nto wind up in raw milk. It is impossible to produce raw milk without\nthose kinds of things happening.'\n\nSmith said the 7 people were sickened from late July to late August\n2010. He said the lag between the illnesses and the department's\nreport Thursday was due to the time needed for the investigation and\nlaboratory tests.\n\nThe department said it has identified 47 other people who became ill\nsince 1 Jan 2010, after drinking raw milk from a variety of other\nsources throughout the state. Most of those cases have been young\nadults or children.\n\nHartmann's farm was implicated as the source of an outbreak of 8\nconfirmed E. coli infections in May and June. In response, the\nMinnesota Department of Agriculture ordered Hartmann to stop selling\nall milk until he remedied the unsanitary conditions on the farm.\nNichole Neeser, who leads the dairy inspection program for the state\nAgriculture Department, said the restriction remained in effect\nThursday. Because of an ongoing investigation of the farm, she said\nshe couldn't comment further.\n\nAlso this summer, the department prohibited the sale of specific lots\nof meat, cheese and other dairy products from the farm. Hartmann\nfought the embargo in Sibley County court. Neeser said arguments\nfinished in late September 2010 and the judge hasn't yet ruled.\n\nFederal and state regulators have said raw milk carries a risk of\ndisease because it hasn't been through the germ-destroying\npasteurization process. However, interest in raw milk has been\ngrowing among those who think pasteurization reduces milk's nutritional value.\n\n9 U.S. states allow retail sales of raw milk, while another 19,\nincluding Minnesota, allow only direct sales from farmers to consumers.\n\n[Byline: Chris Williams]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Classical zoonotic organisms such as _Brucella abortus_, _Brucella\nmelitensis_, _Mycobacterium bovis_, _Salmonella_ species, _Listeria\nmonocytogenes_, _Campylobacter_ species, _Yersinia_ species,\n_Coxiella burnetii_, and _E. coli_ O157:H7 are associated with raw\nmilk ingestion.\n\nNon-zoonotic organisms such as _Streptococcus pyogenes_, _Salmonella_\nTyphi, _Corynebacterium diphtheriae_, _Shigella_ species,\n_Salmonella_ Paratyphi A, _Salmonella_ Paratyphi B, enterotoxins from\n_Staphylococcus aureus_, and hepatitis A have also been associated\nwith raw milk ingestion.\n\nOther diseases that can be related to unpasteurized milk are\nhighlighted in these paragraphs extracted from Leedom JM: Milk and\nInfectious Diseases in Humans. Clin Infect Dis 2006; 43: 610-5\n(<http://www.journals.uchicago.edu/doi/full/10.1086/507035>) with the\ncitations renumbered to be consecutive starting from 1:\n\n'In 1996 and 1998, there were 2 episodes involving rabid cows that\noccurred in Massachusetts (1). Milk from rabid cows can contain\nrabies virus, and transmission via unpasteurized milk is\ntheoretically possible. Temperatures reached during pasteurization\nkill the virus. 80 persons consumed unpasteurized milk that was\ncollected from the 2 cows, and 9 more had contact with saliva from\nthe cows. All 89 persons received postexposure rabies prophylaxis,\nand no human cases of rabies eventuated. A similar report in Oklahoma\nof possible rabies exposure associated with the consumption of raw\nmilk or cream from a rabid cow was circulated in 2006 (2).\n\nTickborne encephalitis, a zoonotic arbovirus infection usually\ntransmitted to humans by the bite of an _Ixodes persulcatus_ or\n_Ixodes ricinus_ tick, is endemic to Central Europe, Eastern Europe,\nand Russia (3). However, the virus can be found in the milk of cows\nand goats with tickborne encephalitis and was reported to be\ntransmissible to humans by the consumption of unpasteurized milk (4).\nA case-control study failed to confirm oral transmission (5).\n\nA diarrhea syndrome (later named Brainerd diarrhea) occurred among\n122 residents of Brainerd, Minnesota, during the period December\n1983-July 1984 (6). It was characterized by acute onset, marked\nurgency, lack of systemic symptoms, failure to respond to\nconventional antimicrobial agents, and a long median duration of\nillness (median duration, 16.5 months). The syndrome was linked to\nconsumption of raw milk from a single dairy (6). No etiologic agent\nwas ever isolated. The outbreak of Brainerd diarrhea stopped when all\nof the dairy's output was diverted and pasteurized (6, MT Osterholm,\npersonal communication).\n\nSubsequent outbreaks in Illinois and Texas were not directly\nassociated with milk, although cattle had been in the vicinity of an\nIllinois well that had its water implicated as a vehicle of\ntransmission (7). Another outbreak of Brainerd-like diarrhea,\nalthough not associated with raw milk, affected 58 (15 percent) of\n394 passengers aboard a cruise ship visiting the Galapagos Islands in\nEcuador (8).\n\nReferences\n----------\n1. CDC: Mass treatment of humans who drank unpasteurized milk from\nrabid cows -- Massachusetts, 1996-1998. JAMA 1999; 281: 1371-2 [available at\n<http://jama.ama-assn.org/cgi/content/full/281/15/1371>].\n2. Rabies, bovine, human exposure - USA (OK). 2006 [ProMED-mail\narchive no. 20060101.0005. Accessed 26 Jul 2006].\n3. Dumpis U, Crook D, Oksi J: Tick-borne encephalitis. Clin Infect\nDis 1999; 28(4): 882-90 [available at\n<http://www.journals.uchicago.edu/doi/pdf/10.1086/515195>].\n4. Snider, DVM, PhD\nAssistant Professor of Pathology\nOSU Center for Veterinary Health Sciences\n<tim.snider@okstate.edu>\n CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - AUSTRALIA (SOUTH AUSTRALIA): CORRECTION\n***********************************************\n", "reports": [{"diseases": ["cryptosporidiosis"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": [], "locations": []}]}, {"archive_id": "9201", "headline": "PRO> Cryptosporidiosis - Australia (SA) (02): comments", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=9201", "date": "2007-03-24 14:00:01", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - AUSTRALIA (SOUTH AUSTRALIA) (02): COMMENTS\n*************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n[1]\nDate: Fri 23 Mar 2007\nFrom: Martyn Kirk <Martyn.Kirk@health.gov.au>\n\nIn the posting regarding _Cryptosporidium_ infections in South\nAustralia the editor indicates that it is not usual for\n_Cryptosporidia_ to be spread from person-to-person. Our experience\nin Australia, is that cryptosporidiosis is commonly spread from one\ninfected person to another as shown by case control studies and the\ncommonness of secondary spread in households (Robertson et al, 2002).\nIt is often assumed that _Cryptosporidium_ is predominantly a\nwaterborne parasitic infection because of well-publicised waterborne\ndisease outbreaks. Waterborne cryptosporidiosis is clearly important\nwhere water supplies have become contaminated by animal waste (Smith\net al, 2006). However, the vast majority of cases of\ncryptosporidiosis reported to health departments are sporadic with no\nknown source.\nIn Australia, outbreaks of waterborne disease of any aetiology are\nrare. In 1998, Sydney residents were advised to boil water due to\nconcerns about contamination of the water supply with\n_Cryptosporidium_, although numbers of oocysts in water were\nsubsequently thought to be overestimated and there were no associated\nhuman infections.\nEvery 4-6 years, State and Territory health departments report major\ncommunity-wide outbreaks of cryptosporidiosis with some clustering of\ncases occurring around swimming pools and petting zoos (Puech et al,\n2001; Ashbolt et al, 2003). These community-wide increases are not\nrelated to water supplies, as they cross multiple water supply\nsystems and a source cannot be identified for the majority of cases.\nIn reality, the 2 main modes of transmission operating for\n_Cryptosporidium sp._ are from infected animals and infected people\nto susceptible individuals. These 2 modes of transmission probably\nreflect the 2 main species infecting humans: _C. hominis_ and _C.\nparvum_ (Hunter et al, 2007).\nReferences:\n1. Robertson B, Sinclair MI, Forbes AB, Veitch M, Kirk M, Cunliffe D,\nWillis J, Fairley CK: Case-control studies of sporadic\ncryptosporidiosis in Melbourne and Adelaide, Australia. Epidemiol\nInfect 2002 Jun;128(3):419-31.\n2. Smith A, Reacher M, Smerdon W, Adak GK, Nichols G, Chalmers RM:\nOutbreaks of waterborne infectious intestinal disease in England and\nWales, 1992-2003. Epidemiol Infect 2006 Dec;134(6):1141-9.\n3. Puech MC, McAnulty JM, Lesjak M, Shaw N, Heron L, Watson JM: A\nstatewide outbreak of cryptosporidiosis in New South Wales associated\nwith swimming at public pools. Epidemiol Infect 2001 Jun;126(3):389-96.\n4. Ashbolt RH, Coleman DJ, Misrachi A, Conti JM, Kirk MD: An outbreak\nof cryptosporidiosis associated with an animal nursery at a regional\nfair. Commun Dis Intell 2003;27(2):244-9.\n5. Hunter PR, Hadfield SJ, Wilkinson D, Lake IR, Harrison FC,\nChalmers RM: Subtypes of _Cryptosporidium parvum_ in humans and\ndisease risk. Emerg Infect Dis 2007 Jan;13(1):82-8.\n--\nMartyn Kirk\nCoordinating Epidemiologist\nOzFoodNet\nOffice of Health Protection\nAustralian Government Department of Health & Ageing\n<Martyn.Kirk@health.gov.au>\n******\n[2]\nDate: Thu 22 Mar 2007\nFrom: Ann Koehler <ann.koehler@health.sa.gov.au>\n\nWe have a severe drought in South Australia at the moment, and\nbelieve that more parents than usual will be bathing their children\nin the same bathwater in order to conserve water.\nI am surprised that you doubt that _Cryptosporidium_ is transmitted\nby sources other than 'water such as a swimming pool'. In our\ninvestigations we had quite a lot of cases where many family members\nwere sick, and where there had been no history of contact with\nswimming or wading pools or other recreational water sources.\nSome children had shared baths, and we believe this was implicated.\nAs _Cryptosporidium_ oocysts are infectious immediately they are\npassed and people have watery diarrhoea, it would be highly unlikely\nthat person-to-person contact transmission does NOT occur -- as it\ndoes with bacterial diarrhoeal illnesses.\nI suggest you refer to the following case reports where\nperson-to-person transmission has been implicated:\n1. Wolfson JS et al: Cryptosporidiosis in immunocompetent hosts. N\nEngl J Med 1985;312:1278-82.\n2. Alpert G et al: Outbreak of cryptosporidiosis in a day-care\ncenter. Pediatrics 1986;77:152-157.\n3. Heijbel H et al: Outbreak of diarrhea in a day care center with\nspread to household members: the role of _Cryptosporidium_. Pediatr\nInfect Dis J 1987;6:532-535.\n4. Cordell RL et al: Cryptosporidiosis in child care settings: a\nreview of the literature and recommendations for prevention and\ncontrol. Pediatr Infect Dis J 1994;13:310-317.\n5. Koch KL et al. Cryptosporidiosis in hospital personnel. Evidence\nfor person-to-person transmission. Ann Intern Med 1985;102:593-596.\nThere are quite a few additional reports of nosocomial outbreaks.\n--\nDr Ann Koehler, BSc MBBS FRCPA MPH\nDirector and Consultant Microbiologist Communicable Disease Control\nBranch Department of Health\nL1/162 Grenfell St\nAdelaide SA 5251\n<ann.koehler@health.sa.gov.au>\n[ProMED-mail thanks Martyn Kirk and Ann Koehler for their comments.\nWe agree that it is well documented that _Cryptosporidia_ can be\ntransmitted from person to person and we hereby pass the information\nand the references to the different studies. - Mod.EP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n In 1998, Sydney residents were advised to boil water due to\nconcerns about contamination of the water supply with\n_Cryptosporidium_, although numbers of oocysts in water were\nsubsequently thought to be overestimated and there were no associated\nhuman infections. Every 4-6 years, State and Territory health departments report major\ncommunity-wide outbreaks of cryptosporidiosis with some clustering of\ncases occurring around swimming pools and petting zoos (Puech et al,\n2001; Ashbolt et al, 2003). Our experience\nin Australia, is that cryptosporidiosis is commonly spread from one\ninfected person to another as shown by case control studies and the\ncommonness of secondary spread in households (Robertson et al, 2002). As _Cryptosporidium_ oocysts are infectious immediately they are\npassed and people have watery diarrhoea, it would be highly unlikely\nthat person-to-person contact transmission does NOT occur -- as it\ndoes with bacterial diarrhoeal illnesses. These community-wide increases are not\nrelated to water supplies, as they cross multiple water supply\nsystems and a source cannot be identified for the majority of cases.\n In our\ninvestigations we had quite a lot of cases where many family members\nwere sick, and where there had been no history of contact with\nswimming or wading pools or other recreational water sources. From: Ann Koehler <ann.koehler@health.sa.gov.au>\n\nWe have a severe drought in South Australia at the moment, and\nbelieve that more parents than usual will be bathing their children\nin the same bathwater in order to conserve water. 'Our research has found children sharing baths have\ncontracted cryptosporidiosis, so we are urging parents with children\nwho are or have been infected, that they make sure these children bathe alone.'\nThe department's water quality adviser David Cunliffe said the\norganism was commonly spread by contact with people already infected\nor from contaminated water or livestock. 'Swimming pools are a\nparticular concern because they can be a source of outbreaks if used\nby people with cryptosporidiosis,' Dr Cunliffe said. 'The organism is\nshed for an extended time after symptoms have ceased and it is\ndifficult to kill by chlorination.' Health authorities said the risk\nof infection could be reduced by washing hands with soap and water\nafter using the toilet, before eating, and after handling animals.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[Spread from person to person is not usual for _Crytosporidia_. 'We had a\nlot of rain for this time of the year -- normally we have some diarrhoea\ncases around this time, but it never turns into an epidemic. The rain is\nthe underlying cause.'\nKutloano Leshomo, Communications Officer for the United Nations Children's\nFund (UNICEF), said laboratory tests of samples from Francistown, conducted\nby the Centres for Disease Control (CDC) in Atlanta, USA, suggested the\noutbreak had come from various sources.\n'Contaminated water, unhygienic practices at the household level, poor\nsanitation, infant feeding-bottle contamination with human waste and\nongoing person-to-person transmission' had all contributed to spreading the\ndisease. The tests indicated the presence of Enteropathogenic _E. coli_\nbacteria and Cryptosporidium.\nWater authorities, 'especially in villages, have acknowledged that they do\nnot have capacity and resources for regular monitoring of the quality of\nwater, meaning that sometimes water is not chlorinated,' Leshomo said.\nBoitshoko pointed out that 'we have contamination in the water, mainly in\nrural areas where the majority of the population lives -- village\nreservoirs and natural ponds have been affected, and people are drinking\nrainwater'.\nThe reason it has taken so long to control the outbreak, Leshomo suggested,\nwas 'first, because the Ministry of Health lacks sufficient capacity to\nhandle an emergency of this nature, especially diarrhoea coupled with\nmalnutrition, but also because some of the issues are outside the\njurisdiction of the Ministry of Health, the water supply authority in the\nvillages, for example, falls under the Ministry of Local Government.'\nAccording to Dr. Dorothy Ochola, Project Officer at UNICEF, 'severe, acute\nmalnutrition, secondary to diarrhoea, is contributing to the high fatality\nrate'. Since the onset of the outbreak, a steady increase in cases of\nmalnutrition-related illnesses, especially marasmus and kwashiorkor, have\nbeen registered. 'It is estimated that approximately 450 to 500 children\nare in urgent need of therapeutic feeding,' she said.\nThe outbreak is widespread and 23 264 cases have been reported across all\nof Botswana's districts. Ministry of Health statistics recorded 4564 cases\nin the southeastern region of Serowe-Palapye, believed to be the origin of\nthe epidemic, and 4391 cases in Kweneng East, the areas that have been hit\nhardest.\nWith the number of weekly cases on the decline -- 1201 reported last week\n-- Boitshoko said the 'epidemic is still not under control but it is going\nin the right direction'. A concerted effort by the World Health\nOrganisation, UNICEF, the International Red Cross, the CDC, Medecins sans\nFrontieres and local water authorities, coordinated by the Ministry of\nHealth, has accelerated the response to the outbreak.\n'The numbers have been going down due to the various interventions that\nhave been put in place, such as concerted social mobilisation to educate\nparents,' Leshomo noted. 'We are now very hopeful,' Boitshoko said. 'We\nshould not have to lose so many children to such a disease. Diarrhoea\nshould not have to kill.'\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[From the more detailed information now available, it is clear that the\ndiarrhoea outbreak is due to general problems with the quality of the water\nsupply in areas of Botswana, where surface water is relied upon for\ndrinking water. Cryptosporidium is certainly one possible etiological\nagent, but _E. coli_ and most probably viruses will also be found. We\nhereby cut the thread on the outbreak of diarrhoea in Botswana. - Mod.EP]", "summary": "The reason it has taken so long to control the outbreak, Leshomo suggested,\nwas 'first, because the Ministry of Health lacks sufficient capacity to\nhandle an emergency of this nature, especially diarrhoea coupled with\nmalnutrition, but also because some of the issues are outside the\njurisdiction of the Ministry of Health, the water supply authority in the\nvillages, for example, falls under the Ministry of Local Government.'
