The outbreak was\nreported to the food safety authorities by the company management on 2 Sep\n2005, following the onset of diarrhoea among a large number of employees\nduring that week (week 35). To investigate the cause of diarrhoea, people\nwith symptoms were urged to submit faecal samples for examination, and a\ncase-control study was initiated. The faecal samples tested negative for\ndiarrhoea-causing viruses and bacteria, but 13 of 17 patients were found by\nmicroscopy to be infected with Cryptosporidium.\nThe parasite was subsequently typed as _Cryptosporidium hominis_ by PCR.\nResults of the case-control study indicated that disease was associated\nwith eating in the company canteen in the 1st part of the previous week\n(week 34). Eating food from the salad bar in the canteen was also\nassociated with disease. A 2nd questionnaire-study was conducted during\nweek 39. This was conducted as a cohort study using an electronic form\ndistributed by email to all employees of the company, and contained\nquestions about the individual food items available from the salad bar.\nA total of 364 out of about 600 employees of the company participated in\nthe 2nd study. Of these, 78 met the case definition (diarrhoea with onset\nafter 20 Aug 2005). Disease was associated with eating in the company\ncanteen on Monday (relative risk [RR]: 4.1, 95 percent confidence intervals\n[CI]: 2.2-7.6) or Tuesday (RR: 4.4, 95 percent CI: 2.2-8.8) in week 34, and\neating from the salad bar on those days (RR: 3.1, 95 percent CI: 2.0-4.8\nand RR: 3.1, 95 percent CI: 2.0-4.7, respectively).\nAn analysis of questionnaire responses from people who reported eating from\nthe salad bar found that 2 food items in particular were associated with\ndisease: whole peeled carrots (RR: 2.4, 95 percent CI: 1.5-4.0) and grated\ncarrots (RR: 2.0, 95 percent CI: 1.2-3.5). In a multivariate analysis, only\nthese 2 food items were associated with disease. The whole carrots were\nconsidered to be a probable vehicle for infection, as they were kept in a\nlarge bowl with water. One hypothesis is that employees using the canteen\nmay have taken carrots from the bowl using their hands, and that this\naction could have contaminated the carrots if any of these people were\nhuman carriers of the parasite. There was no evidence to suggest that the\ncarrots were contaminated by the kitchen staff. As a consequence of the\noutbreak, the canteen management has announced that raw vegetables will no\nlonger be served in water.\nA total of 99 people reported onset of diarrhoea after 20 Aug 2005. Only\n2/3 of the individuals assumed to have been exposed to canteen food during\nthe relevant time period participated in the studies, and so the actual\nnumber of infected patients may have been higher.\nOutbreaks of cryptosporidium infections are uncommon in Denmark. The only\nprevious report was a hospital-associated outbreak in 1989, where the\nlikely source of the outbreak was an ice cube machine contaminated by a\npatient excreting the parasite [1]. In Denmark, cryptosporidium infections\nare not notifiable and faecal samples from patients with gastrointestinal\nsymptoms are not always examined for parasites. Therefore, a reliable\nestimate of the incidence is not available. However, based on the number of\npositive samples reported from the diagnostic laboratory at Statens Serum\nInstitut, the infection is believed to be relatively rare compared with\nother gastrointestinal infections.\nReferences:\nRavn P, Lundgren JD, Kjaeldgaard P, Holten-Anderson W, Hojlyng N, Nielsen\nJO et al. Nosocomial outbreak of cryptosporidiosis in AIDS patients. BMJ\n1991; 302 (6771):277-280.\n[Reported by: Steen Ethelberg (1) (SET@ssi.dk), Morten Lisby (2), Lasse S\nVestergaard (1), Heidi Larsen Enemark (3) and K\ufffd M\ufffdlbak (1). 1). Statens\nSerum Institut, Copenhagen, Denmark 2); Regional Food Control Authority\nNortheast-Sealand, Copenhagen, Denmark; 3). Danish Institute for Food and\nVeterinary Research, Copenhagen, Denmark]\n--\nProMED\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[Infections with cryptosporidia have been extensively reported from the\nUnited Kingdom (see below). The prevalence -- at least of symptomatic\ninfections -- is low in humans in Scandinavia. However, the present report\nshows there are probably carriers who can serve as source of infection\ngiven the opportunity. Serving raw carrots in a bowl of water is common\npractice, but apparently carries a small risk if people serve the carrots\nwith their fingers.\nThe investigation of cases and controls by questionnaire or interview shows\nthe strength of these methods for identifying the source of infection. -\nMod.EP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n An analysis of questionnaire responses from people who reported eating from\nthe salad bar found that 2 food items in particular were associated with\ndisease: whole peeled carrots (RR: 2.4, 95 percent CI: 1.5-4.0) and grated\ncarrots (RR: 2.0, 95 percent CI: 1.2-3.5). However, she asserted that further\ninvestigations and tests are needed to confirm the causative organism.\nMicroscopic parasites of the genus _Cryptosporidium_ cause a type of\ndiarrhoea known as cryptosporidiosis. The parasite lives in the intestines\nof human beings and animals and is usually found in the stool.\nBalosang said investigations at the Centre for Disease Control (CDC) in\nAtlanta, America have found cryptosporidium in 31 of the 55 samples sent\nfrom Francistown. She emphasised hygienic practices such as washing of\nhands with soap at all times. She encouraged people to boil all drinking\nwater regardless of whether it is from the taps or not and to thoroughly\nwash vegetables and fruits. Resources such as bedding, mattresses and back up tents\nhave been dispatched to the districts. She encouraged mothers to\nbreastfeed, emphasising that it is better than formula-feeding. She\ncautioned that bottle feeding require a high level of hygiene. She said\nthat babies are more disadvantaged because of body size and loss of fluids.\nShe called for parents to bring their children to health facilities\nimmediately they show signs of diarrhoea. Health officials reported 1738 cases\nacross 20 counties in western and central New York, up from 746 reported\nillnesses Thursday. Seneca Lake Park's popular Sprayground has been closed\nfor the remainder of the summer as a result.\nSo far, 13 cases in 4 different counties have been confirmed as\ncryptosporidiosis, a common waterborne disease. That's an increase from 5\nconfirmed cases Thursday. Crypto, as it is known, is very contagious and\ncan cause symptoms including diarrhea, nausea, vomiting and fever that can\nlast for weeks.\nThe Health Department initially shut down the Sprayground temporarily\nTuesday after receiving more than 100 reported cases of illness dating back\nto early July 2005. Once the public was notified of the outbreak, the\nnumber of reported illnesses then jumped to more than 700 overnight.\nTests conducted by the Health Department have found the presence of\ncryptosporidium in 2 storage tanks that supply water for the spray park,\nofficials said Friday. Additional water samples from the Seneca Lake State\nPark beach showed no health concerns with the water quality, and the beach\nremains open.\nTo prevent the further spread of illness, the Health Department advises the\nthorough washing of hands after using the toilet, changing diapers or\ncoming in contact with fecal matter in any way. Swimmers should also avoid\nswallowing water, especially in ponds, lakes and rivers, officials said.\nAdditionally, people experiencing gastrointestinal disease and who work in\nfood services, or with children or the sick should stay home from work\nuntil they have recovered. Contaminated drinking water in the Milwaukee area sickened\nmore than 400 000 people in 1993, according to the Centers for Disease Control.\nThe State Health Department said the number of reported illnesses possibly\nlinked to the park is now 3131, with 394 cases confirmed as the intestinal\nillness cryptosporidiosis. Those affected have come from 32 counties across\nthe state, and from several states, Mexico and Canada. Nearly 1000 people\nfrom Ontario County have reported symptoms, along with 417 from Seneca, 650\nfrom Wayne, and 175 from Yates.\n'We recognize this is a significant outbreak, and our priority is to\ncontinue working with counties to increase public awareness about the\nprecautions everyone should take to stop further spread of illness,' said\nstate Health Department spokesman Jeffrey Hammond.\nRecreational water illnesses aren't uncommon, but in his more than 2\ndecades as an area pediatrician, Dr. Robert Weinberg of Geneva can't recall\nseeing a single case of cryptosporidiosis. Symptoms of the illness include\ndiarrhea, abdominal cramping, nausea, vomiting, fever, headache and loss of\nappetite. 'I can't recall a case [of cryptosporidiosis] in children\nbefore,' said Weinberg, a pediatrician with Finger Lakes Medical Associates.\nAlthough the spray park closed on 15 Aug 2005, the number of reported cases\ncontinues to rise, with increased reporting and the spread of the disease\nthrough secondary means, such as contact with an individual carrying the\nhighly infectious disease. But even as the number of confirmed cases\ncontinues to rise, Weinberg suspects the disease will dissipate within the\nnext 3 to 4 weeks. More and more people are becoming aware of how to stop\nits spread, he said, explaining that preventative measures include washing\nhands after using the bathroom and before meals and disinfecting surfaces\nregularly. State health officials have been reluctant to comment on the\ncause of the outbreak, noting that they are still investigating how\ncryptosporidium ended up in 2 storage tanks that supply water to the park.\n[Byline Sujata Gupta <sgupta@fltimes.com>. The pools had been 'shocked,' or subjected to\nunusually high levels of chlorine when not occupied by swimmers. During\nnormal swimming hours, chlorine levels are returned to normal, he said.\nTowler said new posters tell parents young children must wear swim diapers,\nremind parents that diapers cannot be changed on the pool decks, and\nencourage hand washing.\n\n[Byline: Betsy Taylor, AP]\n\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The 2 protozoan parasites Cryptosporidia and Giardia are frequently\ntransmitted in swimming pools. But health authorities are hoping that the outbreak may finally\nbe on the wane, despite the secondary spread to children attending the 2\nunidentified nurseries.\n\nThe outbreak is centred on the Auchingarrich Wildlife Centre, near Comrie,\nand health experts believe that the secondary spread began after a number\nof children from the 2 nurseries, who picked up the infection while\nvisiting the park, passed on the bug to some of their classmates.\n\nA source at NHS Tayside told The Scotsman: 'We have a significant number of\ncases of infection amongst children in a class who did not visit the\ncentre, but there were 2 or 3 children in the class who did visit the\ncentre.' The original outbreak is believed to have started after children\nand adults become infected with the contagious parasite while petting and\nhand-feeding newborn lambs in a barn at the wildlife park. The lambs have\nbeen removed from the park as a precaution.\n\n[Byline: Frank Urquhart]\n\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Person-to-person spread of Cryptosporidiosis is not very common;\nincreasing numbers of cases among people without direct contact with the\nAuchingarrich Wildlife Centre could also suggest spread by drinking water\nand not only person-to-person transmission. - Mod.EP]", "summary": "\n\nThe outbreak is centred on the Auchingarrich Wildlife Centre, near Comrie,\nand health experts believe that the secondary spread began after a number\nof children from the 2 nurseries, who picked up the infection while\nvisiting the park, passed on the bug to some of their classmates. A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Sat, 30 promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Person-to-person spread of Cryptosporidiosis is not very common;\nincreasing numbers of cases among people without direct contact with the\nAuchingarrich Wildlife Centre could also suggest spread by drinking water\nand not only person-to-person transmission.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["cryptosporidiosis"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": [], "locations": [{"location": "Perthshire", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Scotland", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "UK", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2208491", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Cryptosporidiosis, water park - USA (NY)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2208491", "date": "2005-08-19 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS, WATER PARK - USA (NEW YORK)\n******************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nSponsored in part by Elsevier, publisher of\nTravel Medicine and Infectious Disease\n<http://www.travelmedicine.elsevier.com>\n\n[1]\nDate: Fri, 19 Aug 2005\nFrom: Torey Haydon <Teal414@aol.com>\nSource: State of New York [edited]\n<http://www.health.state.ny.us/press/releases/2005/2005-08-17_seneca_lake_release.htm>\n\nState Health Commissioner Antonia C. Novello, M.D., M.P.H., Dr.P.H., today\nissued an update concerning the gastrointestinal illnesses suspected to be\nlinked to a spray park attraction at Seneca Lake State Park. The\nCommissioner also announced the spray park attraction has been closed for\nthe remainder of the 2005 season.\nThe number of reported illnesses possibly linked to the park has grown to\n746 in 8 counties, prompting the Commissioner to again stress precautionary\nmeasures people should take to keep the illness from spreading further. The\n746 cases have been reported by the following counties: Ontario (309\ncases), Seneca (130 cases), Wayne (97 cases), Monroe (75 cases), Cayuga (56\ncases), Livingston (50 cases), Onondaga (24 cases), and Tompkins (5 cases).\nThe most common symptoms reported are diarrhea, abdominal cramping, nausea,\nvomiting, fever, headache and loss of appetite. To date, 5 of the cases\nhave been confirmed as cryptosporidiosis: one in Wayne County, one in\nOntario County and 3 in Cayuga County. Additional tests for other possible\ncauses of illness are pending. Of the 746 cases reported, many people have\nsince recovered, and those who are ill are instructed to see their physician.\nThe reported dates of illness range from early July to the present. The\nState Health Department is working with local health departments and the\nState Office of Parks, Recreation and Historic Preservation to investigate\nthe outbreak. The review is ongoing. Dr. Novello is recommending the\nfollowing precautions:\n1. Persons who visited the Seneca Lake State Park or family members and/or\nclose contacts of ill individuals who visited the park since July and are\nexperiencing gastrointestinal illness should contact their medical provider\nand their local health department (phone numbers below).\n2. Health care workers with patient contact and food workers should notify\ntheir employer if they are experiencing gastrointestinal illness symptoms\nbefore returning to work.\n3. Children and staff with stomach/intestinal illness should refrain from\nattending day care. Day care centers should consult with their local health\ndepartment for recommendations on the timing for return to the day care\nsetting. Children should not be transferred to another day care center.\nDr. Novello also recommends that people take action to minimize the chances\nof acquiring and spreading illness, including thoroughly washing hands\nafter using the toilet, changing diapers, or coming in contact with fecal\nmaterial in any way. Also, swimmers should avoid swallowing water,\nespecially in lakes, ponds, or rivers. To help prevent the spread of\nillness, individuals who are experiencing gastrointestinal illness should\nnot swim while they are sick and for 2 weeks after their symptoms subside.\nIndividuals or family members who visited the State Park and are\nexperiencing gastrointestinal illness should contact their health care\nprovider.\n******\n[2]\nDate: Fri, 19 Aug 2005\nFrom: John Marr <jsmarr4@earthlink.com>\nSource: NY Times [edited]\n<http://www.nytimes.com/2005/08/19/nyregion/19sick.html>\n\nA play area filled with water jets and sprinklers at a state park in\ncentral New York has been identified as the source of a severe intestinal\nillness that has stricken nearly 750 people in 3 weeks, the State Health\nDepartment said yesterday.\nNearly all of the confirmed cases of the disease, a highly contagious\nwaterborne infection called cryptosporidiosis, have so far been traced to\nthe attraction, known as the Sprayground, at Seneca Lake State Park in\nGeneva, N.Y., a Health Department spokesman, Jeffrey W. Hammond, said.\nAlthough none of the people who were infected are known to be critically\nill, the outbreak may be among the largest ever involving cryptosporidiosis\nin New York State, which typically has fewer than 500 cases a year.\nThe Sprayground, which gets about 43 000 visitors every August, was closed\non Monday after health officials noted more than 100 cases of the disease\nin 8 counties. Last night, health officials said tests on a water tank that\nfeeds the play area had tested positive for cryptosporidiosis. The tank,\nwhich uses water supplied by the nearby town of Waterloo, is used solely\nfor the Sprayground and has a chlorination and a filtration system.\nA spokeswoman for the New York State Office of Parks, Recreation and\nHistoric Preservation, Wendy Gibson, said she thought the tank was the only\nsource of contaminated water, and said that a beach in the park would\nremain open.\nHealth officials said, however, that they had not determined the extent of\nthe contamination and that they planned to run tests on drinking fountains\nand other areas of the park. 'Our investigation is ongoing, but so far we\nknow that that there was crypto in that tank,' Mr. Hammond said.\nThe disease, which emerged in the United States in the 1970's, is known to\nbe one of the most frequent causes of traveler's diarrhea. Experts say it\nis contracted primarily through contaminated food and tainted water\nsupplies and spreads very easily. Once it is picked up, the parasite lodges\nitself in the victim's intestines for days or weeks, producing eggs and\ncausing severe abdominal pain, vomiting and diarrhea.\nOutbreaks of the disease are fairly common. One of the worst, in Milwaukee\nin 1993, sickened 400 000 people and killed a half-dozen. But generally the\ndisease is not deadly and lasts only about a week in most people, then goes\naway on its own.\nLast night, health officials advised those who had recently visited the\nSprayground and then experienced stomach problems to see a doctor. They\nstressed that people who suspect they have contracted the disease should\nwash their hands frequently and stay away from others; many cases reported\nso far have occurred in groups, often among people in the same household.\nThe first cases in the 8 counties near Seneca Lake emerged in early July,\nofficials said. After an investigation, they found that most of the victims\nwere children who had played in the Sprayground and complained of nausea\nand diarrhea a short time later. 'We closed the Sprayground down\nimmediately, and it's now going to be closed for the season,' Ms. Gibson said.\nThe largest number of cases, 309 as of last night, was in Ontario County,\nand the bulk of all the cases, 746 as of last night, were confirmed this\nweek after the public was alerted to the outbreak. In Tompkins County, 25\ncases were reported Tuesday and Wednesday -- compared with 2-3 in an\naverage year.\nMs. Gibson said the extent of the outbreak might have to do with the\npopularity of the Sprayground, which is about 11 000 square feet and often\nused by parents as well as children.\n'It's accessible for different ages,' Ms. Gibson said. 'The beauty of it is\nthat it is pretty accessible for people and doesn't involve a pool. It's a\nwhole series of sprinklers.'\n[Byline: Anahad O'Connor, NY Times]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n******\n[3]\nDate: 19 Aug 2005\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: WSTM.com\n<http://www.wstm.com/Global/story.asp?s=3742696>\n\nNumber stricken by water park illness tops 1700\n----------------------\nThe number of people who've reported getting sick after visiting an upstate\nNew York water playground has risen to more than 1700.\nThat's according to officials at the state Department of Health in Albany.\nThe agency says 1738 cases of illness have been reported in 20 counties.\nOfficials have traced the illness to the presence of cryptosporidium in 2\nstorage tanks that supply water to the popular 'sprayground' at a park just\noutside Geneva.\nKnown as crypto, the illness causes diarrhea, cramps, nausea, vomiting and\nheadache.\nSo far, health officials have confirmed 13 cases among those who have\nreported getting ill after visiting the park.\nThe state has shut down the sprayground for the rest of the season.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[ProMED-mail would like to thank Lois Levitan <lcl3@cornell.edu> for\nsubmitting an article with similar material on this outbreak. - Mod.MPP]\n[Such parks distribute water as an aerosol, which apparently is a very\neffective way of disseminating cryptosporidia. From\n1991 to 2002 the annual registered cases varied between 569 (2001) and 982\n(1991). An outbreak of 27 cases was recorded in Ayrshire in 1988. An\noutbreak of over 100 cases in Glasgow during 2002 was ascribed to the\npublic water system. An outbreak of 116 cases was reported in the Grampian\nregion during 2002. There has also been an outbreak linked to a\nGlasgow swimming pool. But the Scottish Centre for Infection and\nEnvironmental Health (SCIEH) said neither of those problems was large\nenough to explain the massive recent rise.\nThe bug causes diarrhoea, stomach cramps, and fever and can be fatal to\nbabies, the old, and the sick. Up to 60 cases have been identified in the\nworst-affected regions, such as Lothian. Only Orkney, Shetland, and the\nWestern Isles have not reported the bug in the past month. SCIEH officials\nhave looked at the pattern of cases to see whether there is any link or\ncommon cause, but found none.\nThe centre says in an official report on the problem: 'Although some recent\ncases may have acquired their infection abroad, the increase cannot be\nexplained by tourists returning to Scotland from Alcudia [the affected\nresort in Majorca]. Cases have been reported from all 12 mainland NHS board\nareas. Some boards have reported as much as 44 percent of this year's\nidentifications during this 4-week period. SCIEH is aware of one recent\noutbreak associated with a swimming pool in the Greater Glasgow area, but\nthis does not account for most of the present rise, the reason for which\nremains unknown.'\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[The geographical distribution of the cases over a wide area point to\ndrinking water as a possible source, which has been reported from Scotland\nby ProMED before, most recently in 2002 (see references below). - Mod.EP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>>\nProMED-mail, a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n >\nSource: Scotland on Sunday website [accessed Sunday 12 Oct 2003][edited]\n<http://www.scotlandonsunday.com/>\n\nCases of a virulent stomach bug have rocketed in Scotland, leaving\nscientists baffled. \nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n In the past month more than 160 confirmed samples of cryptosporidium\ninfection have been reported to the country's main infectious disease\ncentre, more than 3 times the number in the same period in previous years. 'We'll probably start testing soon,'\nsaid Shari Stamer, the city's water quality manager. The number of\ncryptosporidium cases in Lawrence has increased to 15, up from 11 when the\noutbreak was disclosed a week ago today [22 Aug 2003], said Kay Kent,\ndirector of the Lawrence-Douglas County Health Department. There were no\ncases reported in Douglas County in 2002. Federal and state health\nofficials are in Lawrence helping investigate the outbreak.\nStudents at 4 Lawrence elementary schools Thursday were sent home with\nquestionnaires designed to help authorities determine how far the disease\nhas spread -- and whether the outbreak has a common source. 'We've had some\nanecdotal information that there may be more diarrhea in the community, so\nwe're really thinking there might be more cases out there,' Kent said. She\nadded: 'The truth of the matter is we do not know the source.'\nMore than 2/3 of those who contract cryptosporidiosis are children -- and\nKent said Thursday that 11 of the 15 Lawrence victims were younger than 18.\nThe parasite is easily passed in swimming pools, because it is able to\nsurvive outside the body for long periods of time and is resistant to\nlevels of chlorine typically found in pools. In Lawrence, the outbreak was\nserious enough for officials to temporarily shut 4 swimming pools for\ndecontamination.\nThey were closed from Friday until Sunday, some of the hottest days of the\nsummer. Kent said Thursday officials had known since late July 2003 that at\nleast one person with cryptosporidium had been swimming, but they chose not\nto take action at the pools until the number of cases grew larger. The\nclosure orders came after consultation with the federal Centers for Disease\nControl and Prevention and the Kansas Department of Health and Environment.\nShe said there was not a standard number of cases that prompts pool\nclosures. 'One is not it,' she said. 'But it really had to do with the fact\nof the number (of cases), the number that had ... some similar type of\ncontact.'\nWhile the source is unknown, Kent said some suspects were low on the list\nof possible causes. Terrorism, for example, is unlikely. 'We've asked that\nquestion ourselves,' she said. 'Just because of the location of the cases\nand where they occurred, we don't believe so. But we asked that question --\nyou always ask that question these days.'\nKent said the city's water supply was not suspected. 'At this point in\ntime, we have no reason to believe that the general water supply is\nimplicated,' she said. 'We are looking at all risk factors, though.' Stamer\nsaid the city was not required to test the water supply for\ncryptosporidium, but federal officials were expected to order monthly\ntesting by 2004. An effective test only recently became available. Other\ntests of the system are designed to detect drinking water contamination,\nStamer said.\n'Filtration is the barrier for cryptosporidium -- chlorine does not kill\nit,' she said. 'We watch our filter performance, we've done filter\nperformance testing and that type of thing.' Lawrence draws its water from\nClinton Lake and the notoriously dirty Kansas River. Stamer said\ncryptosporidium might be lurking in those waters before arriving at the\ncity's treatment plants. 'I can't tell the public it's not out there, at\nleast in the source water,' she said. 'It's not a sterile environment, we\nknow that. Because of how we handle our business here, at the plants, and\nhow the work is being done, I'm pretty confident the water is safe.' City\npools aren't regularly tested for the parasite in Lawrence or elsewhere,\nsaid Jimmy Gibbs, the city's aquatics supervisor. Such tests would cost\nupwards of $1000 per sample, he said.\n'Right now, there are no basic regulations or standards,' Gibbs said. 'It's\nkind of one of those things that when it does show up, then you react to\nit.' Kent said city pools on Thursday were tested for the parasite for the\nfirst time. It was not known late Thursday when those test results would be\navailable. 4 officials from the CDC and one from KDHE have been in Lawrence\nthis week helping to investigate the source of the outbreak. Questionnaires\nwent to students of 4 randomly selected schools -- Hillcrest, Prairie Park,\nQuail Run, and Sunflower.\nThe cases do not appear to be centered in any particular neighborhood, Kent\nsaid. More than 2/3 of those who contract cryptosporidiosis\nare children.\nThe parasite is easily passed in swimming pools, because it is able to\nsurvive outside the body for long periods of time and is resistant to\nchlorine disinfection. Most cases of the illness caused by cryptosporidium have been\nlinked to day care centers and swimming pools. So far, 99 cases have\nbeen reported.\nNone are linked to public drinking-water supplies, said Gianfranco\nPezzino, epidemiologist with the Kansas Department of Health and\nEnvironment. However, the agency is warning health officials and\nresidents of 24 northeast Kansas counties to look out for symptoms.\nCases have been reported in counties that include 3 of the state's\nlargest population centers: Topeka, Lawrence, and suburban Kansas\nCity. The Sedgwick County Health Department is sending out an alert\nto infection-control nurses at area hospitals and to doctors who\nspecialize in infectious disease. Physicians are being asked to test\nfor the parasite if patients have prolonged watery diarrhea or have\nsymptoms of a food-borne or gastrointestinal illness.\nNo cases have been reported in Sedgwick County. Mansoor Tahir, a\nsenior epidemiologist at the Sedgwick County Health Department, said\nhe does not expect an outbreak in Wichita because most swimming pools\nare closed for the year. Cryptosporidium is a one-cell parasite that\ncauses diarrhea and stomach cramps. Though seldom deadly, it can be a\nthreat to people with weakened immune systems, such as AIDS patients.\nSo far, no one has been hospitalized, Pezzino said.\n3 of the people suffering from the parasite were known to have been\nat the Great Wolf Lodge water park in Wyandotte County. The facility\nwas closed and the water was hyperchlorinated to kill the parasite.\nNormal levels of chlorine in pools will not kill cryptosporidium,\nPezzino said. The first cases of cryptosporidiosis surfaced in late\nJuly 2003 in Lawrence, and the total there had reached 82 Friday. 54\nof those were in people under age 18, and many others were in family\nmembers of children who stayed at day care centers where the parasite\nwas found, said Kay Kent, director of the Lawrence-Douglas County\nHealth Department. 5 of the 17 cases in neighboring counties were\nlinked to the Lawrence cases. In addition to Douglas County, 8 cases\nwere identified in Johnson County, 4 in Shawnee County, 2 each in\nJefferson and Leavenworth counties, and one in Wyandotte County. This\noutbreak occurred in a hotel in Majorca, in which about 2000 guests had\nstayed during the epidemic period (nearly 100 per cent occupation). All the\nguests at the hotel, which is used by only one tour operator, were British\ntourists. The onset date of the first case was 5 July 2003. At present, CNE\nis aware of 391 cases, 214 of which are known to be positive for\ncryptosporidium. The latest numbers of cases reported to CNE by 13 August\nare: 179 confirmed and suspected in England and Wales (75 confirmed and 104\nsuspected cases reported to the Health Protection Agency Communicable\nDisease Surveillance Centre between 1 July and 5 August), 170 in Scotland\n(112 confirmed and 58 suspected cases reported to SCIEH by 13 August) 42 in\nNorthern Ireland (27 confirmed and 15 suspected cases reported to\nCommunicable Disease Surveillance Centre Northern Ireland by 13 August) No\ncase has been found either in the Spanish population of Alcudia or among\nworkers at the hotel. The main symptoms include diarrhoea, nausea,\nvomiting, and fever. CNE is aware of 9 cases admitted to hospital.\nThe regional health authorities in Majorca have carried out an inspection\nof the hotel and control measures have been taken. The hotel's swimming\npool is suspected to be the source of the infection. It was closed on 23\nJuly, and water samples were taken.\nOther control measures have been implemented to avoid transmission from ill\nto healthy people (particularly transmission through babies' nappies) and\nthe hotel's day nurseries have been disinfected. No failures in the\ninfrastructure of the hotel's water supply have been found. Cryptosporidium\noocysts have been found in water from the swimming pool. The swimming pool\nwas emptied and disinfected, and its filters were changed. The local health\nauthorities have allowed the hotel's owners to refill it and use it again.\nThe epidemiological investigation and the control measures are continuing.\nReferences:\nSCIEH. CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS, SWIMMING POOL - SPAIN (MAJORCA) (03)\n*******************************************************\n The regional health authorities in Majorca have carried out an inspection\nof the hotel and control measures have been taken. The hotel's swimming\npool is suspected to be the source of the infection.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "cryptosporidiosis"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-07-23 00:00:00", "2003-07-22 00:00:00", "2003-07-05 00:00:00", "2003-03-24 00:00:00", "2021-08-05 00:00:00", "2021-08-13 00:00:00", "2021-01-03 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Spain", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "England", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Wales", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Scotland", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Northern Ireland", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2204293", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Cryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) (02)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2204293", "date": "2003-08-06 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS, SWIMMING POOL - SPAIN (MAJORCA) (02)\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail, a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Wed 6 Aug 2003\nFrom: Rodney Cartwright <cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n\nUpdate on the number of British tourists with Cryptosporidia infection\n-----------------------------------------------\nThe number of confirmed and reported cases in British tourists, mainly\nchildren, returning from Alcudia is 188. The majority are associated with\none hotel, but there are a few cases associated with other hotels and with\nno history of having visited the main outbreak hotel. 2 possibilities that\nneed to be considered are contamination of the drinking water supply or\ncontamination of a water theme park used as a popular excursion venue.\nReports are being received of a small number of cases associated with other\nresorts in Mallorca and Menorca.\n\n[Prof. Cartwright has provided information on management of swimming pools\nprovided below. - Mod.MPP]\nThe management of swimming pools associated with Cryptosporidiosis or\ncontaminated by Cryptosporidium oocysts; Pilot Guidance for the U.K.\nFederation of Tour Operators Prepared by Prof. Rodney Cartwright August 2003\nThese notes have been prepared for guidance on the actions to be taken when\ncryptosporidium infection is associated with a swimming pool. They do not\ncover action for prevention of contamination such as a faecal accident\npolicy. Many of the procedures recommended should be undertaken only by an\noperative with the appropriate training. The use of high chlorine levels\nis hazardous and the appropriate health and safety precautions must be\nfollowed.\n1. I am unaware of any published procedure for the decontamination of a\nswimming pool contaminated with cryptosporidium oocysts. There are no\npublished studies on the efficacy of any procedures on the disinfection of\nswimming pools against cryptosporidium, although there is anecdotal\nevidence that certain actions have been associated with the cessation of\nfurther cases of cryptosporidiosis.\n2. There are recommendations for a response to a faecal accident. US\nCenters for Disease Control and Prevention in their weekly publication\n(MMWR) of 25 May 2001 includes advice for contamination by a liquid stool.\na. Raise the free available chlorine concentration to 20 mg/L and maintain\nthe pH between 7.2 and 7.5. Ensure this concentration is found throughout\nall co-circulating pools by sampling at least 3 widely spaced locations\naway from return water outlets. This chlorine and pH level should be\nsufficient to inactivate Cryptosporidium and should be maintained for at\nleast 8 hours, equivalent to a CT inactivation value of 9600. A higher or\nlower free available chlorine level/inactivation time can be used as long\nas a CT inactivation value equalling 9600 is maintained for Cryptosporidium\ninactivation. State or local regulators may require higher free available\nchlorine levels in the presence of chlorine stabilizers such as chlorinated\nisocyanurates. If necessary, consult an aquatics professional to determine\nand identify the feasibility, practical methods, and safety considerations\nbefore attempting the hyperchlorination of any pool.\nb. Ensure that the filtration system is operating while the pool reaches\nand maintains the proper free available chlorine concentration during\ndisinfection.\nc. Backwash the filter thoroughly after reaching the CT value. Be sure the\neffluent is discharged directly to waste and in accordance with state or\nlocal regulations. Do not return the backwash through the filter. Where\nappropriate, replace the filter media.\nd. Swimmers may be allowed into the pool after the required CT value has\nbeen achieved and the free available chlorine level has been returned to\nthe normal operating range allowed by the state or local regulatory\nauthority. Maintain the free available chlorine concentration and pH at\nstandard operating levels based on state or local regulations. If\nnecessary, consult state or local regulatory authorities for\nrecommendations on bringing the free available chlorine levels back to an\nacceptable operating range.\nThis guidance assumes a correctly installed and maintained water treatment\nsystem.\n3. The UK Pool Water Treatment Advisory Group (PWTAG) Swimming Pool Water\n-treatment and Quality Standards (1999) following a faecal accident where\nthere is a possibility of cryptosporidium contamination it states and\nrecommends that:\nThe appropriate procedure relies on the fact that the cysts are, in\nprinciple, large enough to be filtered out.\n- The pool is cleared of people immediately.\n- Disinfectant levels are maintained at the top of the recommended range.\n- The pool is vacuumed and swept.\n- Using a coagulant, the water is filtered for 6 turnover cycles (which\ncould well take up to a day, and so might mean closing the pool until the\nnext day).\n- The filter is backwashed.\n- The pool can then be reopened.\nClearly this is a fairly drastic course of action that any pool operator\nwould want to avoid if possible. But if there is a good reason to suspect\nthat Cryptosporidium or Giardia is responsible (certainly if the person\ninvolved has had diarrhoea for some days), it would be the safest\nprocedure. The local consultant in communicable disease control (CCDC)\nshould be notified. A copy of such emergency measures can usefully be\nincluded in the operation and maintenance manual.\n4. In the PWTAG technical guidance note of February 2001 on Cryptosporidium\nconcentrates on coagulation and filtration but does not deal directly with\ndecontamination of a known contaminated pool. They mention chlorine at a\nlevel of 30mg/L for 4 hours as being necessary to kill cryptosporidium\noocysts but make no recommendations on the use of chlorine. Further\ndetailed advice is given on the use of coagulants and filters.\n5. In the UK there is concern on relying on high chlorine levels alone as a\nmeans of disinfection because\na. There are no published data on the effect of chlorine on cryptosporidium\noocysts in a swimming pool. It is understood that the studies that have\nbeen undertaken are in a laboratory setting.\nb. Chlorine is inactivated by organic material and there may be high levels\nof such material in parts of a pool and especially in filters.\nc. It would be necessary for the chlorine to be evenly distributed\nthroughout the pool.\nd. For chlorine to be effective the pH must be carefully controlled (CDC\nrecommend 7.2 - 7.5)\ne. Other chemicals may interfere with the action of the chlorine.\n6. Concerns with filtration include\na. The need for the filters to be operated at the correct pressure and\nwater flow. The optimum operating conditions should be provided by the\nfilter suppliers\nb. The use of sand of the correct particle size in the filters.\nc. Proper maintenance and back washing of the filters.\nd. The time taken for all the pool water to be filtered. Water equivalent\nto the pool volume may pass through in a given time, but the structure of\nthe pool may be such that all the water has not passed through. That is\nthere may be areas of poor circulation in the pool.\n7. Water can be tested for the presence of cryptosporidium\noocysts. Normally 10L grab samples are tested but larger volumes can be\nexamined by the use of a cartridge filter. The volume of water and\ncollection method should be discussed with the test laboratory. Before\nconsidering the appropriate action, an understanding of the water test\nresults is necessary so that misunderstandings do not occur.\na. Cryptosporidium oocysts are not evenly distributed in the\nwater. Therefore the numbers in a sample do not on their own have any\nsignificance. If oocysts are present, that water is contaminated and poses\na potential risk to bathers.\nb. The absence of oocysts in a sample does not mean that there are no\noocysts elsewhere in the pool that could be a hazard to bathers.\nc. All laboratory tests should be undertaken in a laboratory with\naccreditation for cryptosporidium testing in water. The test method should\nbe based on the US EPA Analytical Method 1662 'Cryptosporidium in Water by\nFiltraion/IMS/FA'\nd. The tests undertaken do not distinguish between live and dead\noocysts. As a rule of thumb they should all be regarded as alive and\npotentially infectious.\ne. If a suspect pool has negative test results for oocysts, further samples\n-- including a backwash and a sample of sand from the top 30cm of the\nfilter -- should be examined.\nf. Examining the deposit from the pump strainer is also worth considering.\n8. The following scenarios are being met associated with hotel pools.\na. 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families using a\nswimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses with\ndiarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. Pool water\nsamples containing cryptosporidium oocysts\nb. As for (a) with no oocysts demonstrated in water samples.\nc. Oocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea.\n9. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool\nusers. Oocysts found in water tests\na. Empty the pool\nb. Thoroughly clean all pipe work and associated tanks\nc. Replace the sand in the filters\nd. Refill the pool and chlorinate to 20 mg/L at pH 7.2 - 7.5 for 8 hours\n10. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. No\noocysts found in water tests\na. Test further water samples and especially backwash samples. Test\nsamples of sand taken from the top 30 cm of the sand filters. If negative\nand further cases do not occur, no further action need be taken and another\nsource of infection should be sought.\nb. Look for non-pool source(s) of infection. This should involve the local\npublic health authorities and the Water Company or companies.\nc. If further cases occur and the pool is the probable source of infection,\nfollow the scenario below, even if oocyst are not found in the pool water\ntests.\n11. Scenario\nOocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea\na. Take further samples including sand from the filters.\nb. Close the pool and any other pools whose water treatment is linked to\nthe fouled pool.\nc. Maintaining disinfectant levels at the top of the operating range,\nvacuum and sweep the pool.\nd. Using optimised coagulation, filter for 6 turnover cycles (which may\nmean closing the pool for a day). This assumes good hydraulics and filters\nthat have been correctly installed and maintained.\ne. Backwash the filters individually, NOT through other filters.\nf. Check free chlorine residuals and pH and if they are satisfactory reopen\nthe pool.\n12. General Considerations in all scenarios\na. Ensure that any water used to fill or top up the pool is free from\nCryptosporidium\nb. Ensure that the pool operation is in accordance with sound water\ntreatment principles as for example in the PWTAG Swimming Pool Water\n-Treatment and Quality Standards (1999)\nc. Consider other pools or water features that may be contaminated.\nd. Do not move guests from a pool with a contaminated pool to another\nhotel. This may spread any infection.\ne. Ensure that all test results are made available to the local public\nhealth authorities\nReferences:\nCDC Guidelines:\nPWTAG:\nSWIMMING POOL WATER - Treatment and Quality Standards (ISBN 0 951 70076 6).\nObtainable through:\nSee also Institute of Sport and Recreation Management:\n\nThese pilot guidelines have been discussed with other workers in the U.K.\nwho have experience in the management of swimming pool outbreaks, and there\nis a general agreement on the contents.\n--\nProf. Rodney Cartwright,\nMicroDiagnostics (UK) Ltd,\nGuildford, England\n<cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n[ProMED thanks Professor Rodney Cartwright for the thorough review of the\nexisting guidelines for cleaning swimming pools and the update on the\nnumber of infected UK tourists. We have not received any reports from other\ncountries of residents at the particular hotel who have diarrhoea. - Mod.EP]", "summary": "e. The incubation period was\nassumed to be within a range of 2 to 10 days, and duration of the illness\nwas between several days and 2 weeks.\nThe recruits were stationed in barracks with a permanent kitchen catered\nfor by a cook. The meals were transported into the field in insulating\ncontainers or eaten in the barracks. Drinks included mainly bottled mineral\nwater, juice concentrate diluted with tap water, and tea. The menu included\nvarious hot and cold meals and leafy and mixed salads. During the exercise\nthe weather was very hot and no rain fell. Sheep graze regularly on the field.\nAnalyses of stool specimens helped to rule out bacterial pathogens, such as\n_Salmonella_, _Yersinia_, _Shigella_, [pathogenic] _Escherichia coli_, or\n_Bacillus cereus_ only a few days into the investigation. Cryptosporidia\nwere found. In 15 of 217 the stool specimens requested after from recovered\nand still ill participants in the exercise, and _Cryptosporidium_ enzyme\nlinked immunosorbent assay found _Cryptosporidium_ antigen. These findings\nwere confirmed by immunofluorescence assay and microscopy.\nThe low number of positives may be due to the fact that the specimens were\ntaken only 2 to 3 weeks after onset of illness. Most people did not have\ngastrointestinal complaints any more and therefore would not have excreted\noocysts. Analysis of food samples did not show any fecal or bacterial\ncontamination. No investigation for _Cryptosporidium_ was performed because\nat this point no parasites were taken into consideration.\nThe result of the microbiological analysis of the drinking water on the\nsite was inconclusive. A second analysis of the reservoir for drinking\nwater and the kitchen water supply for cryptosporidia (filtration of 300\nlitres) and of 5 liters of mineral water imported from Italy yielded no\nresults, as did an examination of sheep droppings.\nReference:\n----------\nRKI. A number\nof cases of Cryptosporidium have been confirmed in Co. Westmeath.\nThese may be linked to the public water supply served by Lough Owel.\nAs a precautionary measure, pending investigation of the possible\nsource of contamination, a boil water notice is being issued.\nAccordingly, in the interest of public health, it is recommended that all\nusers on the scheme boil the water before use.\nWater should NOT be used unboiled for: drinking, drinks made with\nwater, food preparation, brushing of teeth.\nAffected Areas:\nMullingar and surrounding area\nTaughmon/Crookedwood\nCloghan Killucan Raharney Kinnegad\nFrewin to Irishtown and Longford Road to Ballinafid.\nGainstown, Gaybrook, Knockaville, Coralstown, Milltownpass,\nMahonstown/Gibbonstown, Rochfortbridge.\nBelvedere, Dalystown, Tyrrellspas\nBallinea, Dysart, Castletown-Geoghegan, Ballyhast, Ballinagore,\nKilbeggan, Horseleap.\nBrotenstown, Rathconrath, Skeagh.\nFrewin, Ballinacarrigy, Moyvore, Ballymore.\nBunbrosna, Ballinalack, Rathowen, Rathaspic, Streete, Lismacaffrey.\nImportant Points:\nWater can be used for personal hygiene, bathing, and flushing toilets.\nCaution should be taken when bathing children to ensure they do not\ningest the water. Bring water to the boil and allow to cool. Cover and\nstore in a refrigerator or cold place. Water from the hot tap is not safe\nto drink.\nDomestic water filters will not render water safe to drink. Discard ice\ncubes in fridges and freezers. Make ice from cooled boiled water. LaBudde' <ral@lcfltd.com>\nSource: FSNET\n\nPARASITE IN POOL INFECTS 51 PEOPLE AT SUMMER PARTY\n27 Sep 1998\nOregon Live\nDavid Austin\nAn outbreak of cryptosporidiosis in Sellwood, Oregon has afflicted 51\npeople after attending a swimming party at Sellwood Pool, and a number of\nthem remain ill from the incident.\nThe story says that no one was hospitalized from the outbreak,\nand because summer is over the pool has closed. Four\nvictims sought medical treatment from doctors right away, and many of those\nwho attended the event still are hampered by diarrhea, vomiting and stomach\ncramps, fever and flulike symptoms.\nAccording to state health statistics, the outbreak eclipses the largest\nnumber of cryptosporidiosis cases ever recorded in one year in Oregon,\nwhich was the 33 in 1997.\nDr. Gary Oxman, director of Multnomah County's Health Department, was cited\nas suspecting that someone who was carrying the parasite might have\nrelieved himself in the pool, and that chlorine, the common agent used in\nswimming pools to kill bacteria, isn't strong enough to neutralize the\nparasite.\nThe story adds that about 65 people attended the 30 Aug pool party that\nwas sponsored by the Parent Teacher Association at Edwards School in\nSoutheast Portland.\nSchool officials said that at least two students have been absent because\nof the illness and that several adults still are dealing with its effects.\n...................................jw\n--\nDr Jack Woodall\nDirector, ProMED-mail\nInstitute of Biomedical Sciences\nFederal University of Rio de Janeiro\nRio de Janeiro - RJ, Brazil\n<woodall@bioqmed.ufrj.br>\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Canada (British Columbia)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2193217", "date": "1998-04-27 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - CANADA (BRITISH COLUMBIA)\n*********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nDate: Fri, 24 Apr 1998 17:38:52 -0400\nFrom: Robert A. LaBudde <ral@lcfltd.com>\nSource: FSNET\n\nAccording to this story, the city of Chilliwack, B.C., won an award last\nyear for the cleanliness of its water. This week, 35,000 residents of the\nFraser Valley municipality were ordered to boil their tap water because of\nten cases cryptosporidiosis among residents of Chilliwack since Wednesday.\nHealth officials were cited as saying that the final number of confirmed\ninfections may be ten times higher.\nGeorge Rice, a senior health officer with the Fraser Valley Health Region,\nwas quoted as saying the infection caused by the tiny parasite 'is usually\nmild' and involves diarrhea, stomach cramps and vomiting over several days.\nThe story adds that public health warnings have prompted dozens of calls\nfrom other residents reporting the same symptoms, but there have been no\nreports of more serious illness or harm to people already suffering from\nillnesses or weakened immune systems.\nThe town system is usually fed by deep underground wells with occasional\nsupplements from mountain streams. City engineer Andrew Henry was cited as\nsaying it is suspected that feces from an infected animal entered the city\nsystem from a stream which was being used to supplement the city reservoirs\nlast week.\nThe suspect stream source has been shut off and the deep-well water is\nbeing used to flush the entire city's piping system.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n[While livestock faeces can contaminate local water supplies, the recent\nhistory of this disease is that contamination is more likely from defects in\nmunicipal water handling and chlorination procedures. Either way, diarrhea\nfollows. - Mod.MHJ]\n..................................mhj/es\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Australia (New South Wales)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2192806", "date": "1998-02-26 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - AUSTRALIA (NEW SOUTH WALES)\n***********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post", "summary": "", "reports": [{"diseases": ["cryptosporidiosis"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": [], "locations": []}]}, {"archive_id": "2190336", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Cryptosporidiosis - England (South-East)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2190336", "date": "1997-03-10 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - ENGLAND (SOUTH-EAST)\n========================================\nDate: Mon, 10 Mar 1997 18:01:04\nFrom: jchin <jchin@cdpc.com>\n\nCDPC E-mail Service - March 10, 1997\nSource: March 10, 1997 -Communicable Disease Report - UK - Vol 7 No. 10\nClusters of cryptosporidiosis in south east England:\nA total of 85 cases of cryptosporidiosis have been reported to the PHLS\nCommunicable Disease Surveillance Centre (CDSC) from four districts\nwithin the water distribution zone that covers West Hertfordshire, Brent\nand Harrow, part of north Hillingdon, and Barnet since 25 February 1997.\nCryptosporidial oocysts were found in samples of\ntreated water supplying the area. The water company\nissued a 'boil water' notice dated 2 March to 300 000 properties.\nA team has been set up to manage this incident. CDSC (Anglia and Oxford)\nis coordinating the epidemiological studies.\nThree clusters reported in Bedfordshire and Hertfordshire since the\nmiddle of January 1997 have been investigated by local outbreak teams.\nNo common factors were identified among cases. The water supplies to the\nareas concerned are apparently distinct. Cryptosporidial oocysts were\nnot detected in any of the three water distribution systems supplying\nthe affected areas. The water companies rerouted supplies to two of the\naffected areas and introduced other precautionary measures. The cluster\nin Welwyn Garden City and Hatfield consisted of ten cases who became ill\nbetween 15 January and 7 February; no other primary cases were reported\nin February. The other clusters were in Bedford and in Luton.\nOn 5 March, cryptosporidium-like oocysts were detected in one sample of\ntreated water in the Luton area and a 'boil water' notice was issued as\na precaution.\nCryptosporidiosis is usually self limiting, but severely\nimmunocompromised people may develop severe and life threatening\ndisease.\nDr Lorna Willocks, regional epidemiologist for Anglia and Oxford\n(tel 01223 331865; fax 01223 330345) would welcome reports of\ncases of cryptosporidiosis that may be associated with these clusters.\n[see: Cryptosporidium FAQ - ask majordomo for: get promed cryptosporidium]\n...........................................................jw\n--\nJack Woodall, Ph.D., Director, ProMED-mail\nWadsworth Center, New York State Dept. of Health, Albany NY\njack.woodall@wadsworth.org\nhttp://www.fas.org/promed/\nhttp://www.healthnet.org/promed.html\nhttp://www.outbreak.org/cgi-unreg/dynaserve.exe/credits.html#jack\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed.
